3.3, whereas Section 3.4 provides more information on the general
unfairness test.

At the same time, the Court has repeatedly
insisted <a href="#ntr138-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0138"
id="ntc138-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0138">(<span
class="super note-tag">138</span>)</a> that, while its role is to give
guidance on the interpretation of transparency and unfairness,
<span class="bold">it is for the national authorities, in particular the
national courts, to assess the transparency and unfairness of specific
contract terms in light of the specific circumstances of each
case</span>. The Court <a href="#ntr139-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0139"
id="ntc139-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0139">(<span
class="super note-tag">139</span>)</a> has expressed this in the
following way:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘42</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>While it is true that the Court, in
exercising the jurisdiction conferred on it by Article 234 EC <a
href="#ntr140-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0140"
id="ntc140-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0140">(<span
class="super note-tag">140</span>)</a>, in Océano Grupo Editorial and
Salvat Editores, paragraph 22, interpreted the general criteria used by
the Community legislature in order to define the concept of unfair
terms, it cannot however rule on the application of those general
criteria to a particular term, which must be considered in the light of
the particular circumstances of the case in question (see Freiburger
Kommunalbauten, paragraph 22).</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>43</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>It is for the national court, in the light
of the foregoing, to assess whether a contractual term may be
categorised as unfair within the meaning of Article 3(1) of the
Directive.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

It is for the national court to determine whether, having regard to the
particular circumstances of the case, a term meets the requirements of
good faith, balance and transparency.

The same is true with regard to the examination of whether a contract
terms falls within the concept of ‘main subject matter of the contract’
or whether its examination relates to ‘the adequacy of the price and
remuneration’ within the meaning of Article 4(2) of the
UCTD <a href="#ntr141-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0141"
id="ntc141-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0141">(<span
class="super note-tag">141</span>)</a>.

In light of the above, the
Court <a href="#ntr142-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0142"
id="ntc142-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0142">(<span
class="super note-tag">142</span>)</a> has generally refrained from
providing a final assessment of the unfairness of a specific contract
term, leaving this assessment to the referring national court. However,
in certain cases, the Court has nevertheless provided fairly clear
indications as to the unfairness of a given contract
term <a href="#ntr143-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0143"
id="ntc143-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0143">(<span
class="super note-tag">143</span>)</a>.

National courts may develop more specific criteria for the assessment of
the unfairness of contract terms, as long as they comply with the
methodology established by the
Court <a href="#ntr144-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0144"
id="ntc144-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0144">(<span
class="super note-tag">144</span>)</a>. Insofar as, in the interest of
ensuring uniform interpretation of the law, national supreme courts
adopt binding decisions concerning the modalities for implementing the
UCTD, such decisions may not prevent individual courts from ensuring the
full effect of that directive and from offering consumers an effective
remedy, nor from requesting the Court to provide a preliminary
ruling <a href="#ntr145-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0145"
id="ntc145-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0145">(<span
class="super note-tag">145</span>)</a>.

This Notice cannot cover the abundant case law on the assessment of
particular types of contract terms in the Member States.

3.2.   <span class="bold">Contract terms relating to the main subject
matter of the contract or the price and remuneration (Article 4(2)
UCTD)</span>

Contract terms relating to the main subject matter of the contract or
the price and remuneration are within the scope of the
UCTD <a href="#ntr146-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0146"
id="ntc146-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0146">(<span
class="super note-tag">146</span>)</a>. The particularity of such
contract terms is that, under the minimum standard of Article 4(2)
UCTD <a href="#ntr147-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0147"
id="ntc147-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0147">(<span
class="super note-tag">147</span>)</a>, the assessment of their
unfairness under Article 3(1) is
excluded <a href="#ntr148-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0148"
id="ntc148-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0148">(<span
class="super note-tag">148</span>)</a> or
limited <a href="#ntr149-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0149"
id="ntc149-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0149">(<span
class="super note-tag">149</span>)</a> if they are drafted in plain,
intelligible language or, in other words, if such terms meet the
transparency requirements of the UCTD.

Since Article 4(2) of the UCTD lays down an exception to the application
of the unfairness-test under Article 3(1), that provision must be
strictly interpreted <a href="#ntr150-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0150"
id="ntc150-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0150">(<span
class="super note-tag">150</span>)</a>. Article 4(2) must also be
interpreted uniformly throughout the European Union, taking into account
the purpose of the UCTD <a href="#ntr151-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0151"
id="ntc151-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0151">(<span
class="super note-tag">151</span>)</a>. It is for national courts to
determine in individual cases whether a given contract term (a) relates
to the definition of the main subject matter of the contract or whether
the examination of its unfairness would imply an assessment of the
adequacy of the price and
remuneration <a href="#ntr152-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0152"
id="ntc152-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0152">(<span
class="super note-tag">152</span>)</a>, and (b) whether such contract
terms are drafted in plain intelligible
language <a href="#ntr153-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0153"
id="ntc153-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0153">(<span
class="super note-tag">153</span>)</a>.

3.2.1.   <span class="italic">Contract terms relating to the definition
of the main subject matter of the contract</span>

The Court has stated that contract terms falling within the concept of
the ‘main subject matter of the contract’, within the meaning of Article
4(2) of the UCTD, must be understood as being <span class="bold">those
that lay down the essential obligations of the contract and, as such,
characterise it</span> <a href="#ntr154-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0154"
id="ntc154-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0154">(<span
class="super note-tag">154</span>)</a>. By contrast, terms that are
merely <span class="bold">ancillary</span> cannot fall within the
concept of ‘main subject matter of the
contract’ <a href="#ntr155-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0155"
id="ntc155-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0155">(<span
class="super note-tag">155</span>)</a>. In order to determine whether a
term falls within the concept of the ‘main subject matter of the
contract’ the nature, the general scheme and the stipulations of the
contract and its legal and factual context have to be
considered <a href="#ntr156-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0156"
id="ntc156-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0156">(<span
class="super note-tag">156</span>)</a>.

The Court <a href="#ntr157-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0157"
id="ntc157-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0157">(<span
class="super note-tag">157</span>)</a> has expressed this as follows in
relation to foreign currency loans:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘37</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>In the present case, a number of elements in
the documents before the Court indicate that a term, […], incorporated
into a loan agreement concluded in a foreign currency between a seller
or supplier and a consumer without being individually negotiated, on
terms by which the loan must be repaid in the same currency, is covered
by the notion of “main subject matter of the contract” within the
meaning of Article 4(2) of Directive 93/13.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>38</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>[…] the fact that a loan must be repaid in a
certain currency relates, in principle, not to an ancillary repayment
arrangement, but to the very nature of the debtor's obligation, thereby
constituting an essential element of a loan agreement.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

In this regard, the Court <a href="#ntr158-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0158"
id="ntc158-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0158">(<span
class="super note-tag">158</span>)</a> has stressed the difference
between contract terms stipulating that the loan has to be repaid in the
same foreign currency in which it was issued and contract terms under
which a loan dominated in foreign currency had to be repaid in the
national currency according to the selling rate of exchange applied by
the bank <a href="#ntr159-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0159"
id="ntc159-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0159">(<span
class="super note-tag">159</span>)</a>. The Court
considered <a href="#ntr160-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0160"
id="ntc160-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0160">(<span
class="super note-tag">160</span>)</a> that a contractual term,
incorporated into a loan agreement denominated in a foreign currency,
according to which the loan must be repaid in the same foreign currency
as that in which it was contracted, lays down an essential obligation
characterising that contract. It thus relates to the ‘main subject
matter of the contract’ within the meaning of Article 4(2). In that
respect, it is irrelevant if the amount of the loan is made available to
the consumer in local currency and not in the currency stipulated in the
contract <a href="#ntr161-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0161"
id="ntc161-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0161">(<span
class="super note-tag">161</span>)</a>. By contrast, the Court
considered a term defining the currency conversion mechanism to be an
ancillary arrangement <a href="#ntr162-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0162"
id="ntc162-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0162">(<span
class="super note-tag">162</span>)</a>.

3.2.2.   <span class="italic">Contract terms relating to the price and
remuneration</span>

Terms relating to the price and remuneration, i.e. the financial
obligations of the consumer, <span class="bold">are, in principle,
subject to the unfairness test under Article 3(1)</span>. However, under
Article 4(2) <a href="#ntr163-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0163"
id="ntc163-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0163">(<span
class="super note-tag">163</span>)</a>, the unfairness test may include
an assessment of the <span class="italic">adequacy</span> of the price
and remuneration, or, as expressed in Recital 19, of ‘the quality/price
ratio of the goods or services supplied’, only where the relevant terms
are not transparent. By contrast, the unfairness of other aspects
relating to the price or remuneration, such as the possibility of or the
mechanism for unilateral price changes, is to be assessed even if the
relevant terms are fully transparent.

The Court <a href="#ntr164-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0164"
id="ntc164-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0164">(<span
class="super note-tag">164</span>)</a> has described the limitation in
the assessment of such contract terms in the following way in relation
to a loan contract:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘Terms relating to the consideration due by the consumer to the
lender or having an impact on the actual price to be paid to the latter
by the consumer thus do not, in principle, fall within the second
category of terms, except as regards the question whether the amount of
consideration or the price as stipulated in the contract is adequate as
compared with the service provided in exchange by the lender.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court <a href="#ntr165-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0165"
id="ntc165-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0165">(<span
class="super note-tag">165</span>)</a> has further clarified that
contract terms on <span class="italic">price changes</span> are fully
subject to the unfairness test under Article 3(1):

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] However, this exclusion cannot apply to a term relating to a
mechanism for amending the prices of the services provided to the
consumer.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

This is consistent with the fact that the Annex to the UCTD sets out
conditions which terms on price changes normally have to meet in order
not to be considered unfair <a href="#ntr166-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0166"
id="ntc166-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0166">(<span
class="super note-tag">166</span>)</a>.

Furthermore, the Court considers that the fact that a certain fee should
have been included in the calculation of the total cost of a consumer
loan under Directive 2008/48/EC does not indicate that the contract term
setting out that fee is covered by Article 4(2)
UCTD <a href="#ntr167-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0167"
id="ntc167-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0167">(<span
class="super note-tag">167</span>)</a>.

Finally, the Court has clarified that the adequacy of the price or
remuneration is excluded from the unfairness-assessment only if the
relevant terms lay down a real remuneration for a product or service
provided <a href="#ntr168-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0168"
id="ntc168-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0168">(<span
class="super note-tag">168</span>)</a>. On this basis the
Court <a href="#ntr169-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0169"
id="ntc169-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0169">(<span
class="super note-tag">169</span>)</a> has ruled

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] that the exclusion cannot apply to terms that […] merely
determine the conversion rate of the foreign currency in which the loan
agreement is denominated, in order to calculate the repayment
instalments, without however any foreign exchange service being supplied
by the lender in making that calculation and do not, therefore,
constitute “remuneration”, the adequacy of which as consideration for a
service supplied by the lender could be assessed to determine its
unfairness pursuant to Article 4(2) of Directive 93/13.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

3.3.   <span class="bold">Transparency requirements</span>

3.3.1.   <span class="italic">Transparency requirements under the
UCTD</span>

The transparency requirements of the UCTD <span class="bold">apply to
<span class="italic">all</span> types of</span> (not individually
negotiated <a href="#ntr170-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0170"
id="ntc170-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0170">(<span
class="super note-tag">170</span>)</a>) <span class="bold">contract
terms</span> that are within the scope of the
UCTD <a href="#ntr171-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0171"
id="ntc171-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0171">(<span
class="super note-tag">171</span>)</a>.

The Court has interpreted the <span class="bold">requirement in Articles
4(2) and 5</span> according to which contract terms have to be in plain,
intelligible language, <span class="bold">broadly</span>. In this
connection, the Court also has taken into account that, under point 1(e)
of the Annex to the UCTD, the fact that consumers had no real
opportunity of becoming acquainted with a contract
term <a href="#ntr172-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0172"
id="ntc172-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0172">(<span
class="super note-tag">172</span>)</a> is an indication of their
unfairness.

Although the Court has not specifically addressed many of the factors
mentioned below, in the Commission's view, the following factors will be
relevant for assessing whether a given contract term is plain and
intelligible within the meaning of the UCTD:

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | whether the consumer had the real opportunity of becoming acquainted with a contract term before the conclusion of the contract; this includes the question of whether the consumer had access to and was given the opportunity to read the contract term(s); where a contract term refers to an annex or to another document, the consumer must have access also to those documents; |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the comprehensibility of the individual terms, in light of the clarity of their wording and the specificity of the terminology used, as well as, where relevant, in conjunction with other contract terms <a href="#ntr173-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0173"
id="ntc173-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0173">(<span
class="super note-tag">173</span>)</a>. In this connection, the position or perspective of consumers to whom the relevant terms are addressed has to be taken into account <a href="#ntr174-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0174"
id="ntc174-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0174">(<span
class="super note-tag">174</span>)</a>; this will also include the question of whether the consumers to whom the relevant terms are addressed are sufficiently familiar with the language in which the terms are drafted; |

<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>the way in which contract terms are presented.
This might include aspects such as:</p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>the clarity of the visual presentation,
including font size,</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>the fact of whether a contract is structured in
a logical way and whether important stipulations are given the
prominence they deserve and are not hidden amongst other
provisions,</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>or whether terms are contained in a contract or
context where they can reasonably be expected, including in conjunction
with other related contract terms etc.</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

For example, contract terms whose impact can only be understood when
reading them jointly, should not be presented in such a way that their
joint impact is obscured, e.g. through placing them in different parts
of the contract <a href="#ntr175-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0175"
id="ntc175-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0175">(<span
class="super note-tag">175</span>)</a>.

The Court has drawn from Articles 4(2) and 5, sometimes referring also
to Recital 20 and the Annex to the UCTD, in particular Points 1(i) and
(j), transparency standards, including information requirements, which
go beyond the aspects referred to above. In that respect, the Court also
uses the term ‘substantive transparency
requirements’ <a href="#ntr176-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0176"
id="ntc176-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0176">(<span
class="super note-tag">176</span>)</a>. According to the Court,
transparency <span class="italic">requires more than contract terms
being formally and grammatically intelligible</span> and
<span class="italic">implies that consumers must be able to evaluate the
economic consequences</span> of a contract term or
contract <a href="#ntr177-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0177"
id="ntc177-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0177">(<span
class="super note-tag">177</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘44</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>As regards <span class="bold">the
requirement of transparency of contractual terms</span>, as is clear
from Article 4(2) of Directive 93/13, the Court has ruled that that
requirement, also repeated in Article 5 thereof, <span
class="bold">cannot be reduced merely to their being formally and
grammatically intelligible</span>, but that, to the contrary, since the
system of protection introduced by Directive 93/13 is based on the idea
that the consumer is in a position of weakness vis-à-vis the seller or
supplier, in particular as regards his level of knowledge, that
requirement of plain and intelligible drafting of contractual terms and,
therefore, <span class="bold">the requirement of transparency laid down
by the directive must be understood in a broad sense</span> […] <a
href="#ntr178-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0178"
id="ntc178-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0178">(<span
class="super note-tag">178</span>)</a>.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div>
<div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘45</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Therefore, the requirement that a
contractual term must be drafted in plain intelligible language is to be
understood as requiring also that the contract should set out
transparently the specific functioning of the mechanism to which the
relevant term relates and the relationship between that mechanism and
that provided for by other contractual terms, <span class="bold">so that
that consumer is in a position to evaluate</span>, on the basis of
clear, intelligible criteria, <span class="bold">the economic
consequences</span> for him which derive from it […] <a
href="#ntr179-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0179"
id="ntc179-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0179">(<span
class="super note-tag">179</span>)</a>.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

This broad understanding of transparency entails that sellers and
suppliers have to provide clear information to consumers on contract
terms and their implications/consequences before the conclusion of the
contract. The Court has repeatedly emphasised the importance of such
information so that consumers can understand the extent of their rights
and obligations under the contract before being bound by it. The
Court <a href="#ntr180-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0180"
id="ntc180-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0180">(<span
class="super note-tag">180</span>)</a> has stated that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] it is settled case-law that <span class="bold">information,
before concluding a contract,</span> on the terms of the contract and
the consequences of concluding it, <span class="bold">is of fundamental
importance for a consumer</span>. It is on the basis of that information
in particular that he decides whether he wishes to be bound by the terms
previously drawn up by the seller or supplier […] <a
href="#ntr181-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0181"
id="ntc181-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0181">(<span
class="super note-tag">181</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court has specified the requirements further, in particular with
regard to contract terms which are essential for the extent of the
obligations consumers accept to undertake, for instance with regard to
contract terms relevant for establishing the payments which consumers
have to make under a loan contract. Some of those rulings concern in
particular mortgage credit contracts (denominated) in a foreign currency
or indexed to a foreign currency. The Court has summarised the standard
to be expected from sellers and suppliers as
follows <a href="#ntr182-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0182"
id="ntc182-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0182">(<span
class="super note-tag">182</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] it is for the national court, when it considers all the
circumstances surrounding the conclusion of the contract, to ascertain
whether, in the case concerned, <span class="bold">all the information
likely to have a bearing on the extent of his commitment have been
communicated to the consumer, enabling him to estimate in particular the
total cost of his loan</span>.</p>
<p>First, whether the terms are drafted in plain intelligible language
enabling an average consumer, that is to say a reasonably well-informed
and reasonably observant and circumspect consumer to estimate such a
cost and,</p>
<p>second, the fact related to the failure to mention in the loan
agreement the information regarded as being essential with regard to the
nature of the goods or services which are the subject matter of that
contract</p>
<p>play a decisive role in that assessment […] <a
href="#ntr183-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0183"
id="ntc183-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0183">(<span
class="super note-tag">183</span>)</a>’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court has applied these standards, for instance, to the functioning
of the currency conversion mechanisms applying to mortgage loans indexed
to a foreign currency <a href="#ntr184-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0184"
id="ntc184-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0184">(<span
class="super note-tag">184</span>)</a> and to the interests and fees
due, including their adaptation, under a consumer credit
agreement <a href="#ntr185-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0185"
id="ntc185-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0185">(<span
class="super note-tag">185</span>)</a>. Furthermore, the Court has
applied these transparency standards to the fact that, in relation to
loans taken out in foreign currencies, consumers bear the risk of the
depreciation of the currency in which they receive their
income <a href="#ntr186-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0186"
id="ntc186-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0186">(<span
class="super note-tag">186</span>)</a>. Such depreciation may indeed
affect their ability to pay back the loan. In such cases, the Court
requires the seller or supplier to set out the possible variations in
the exchange rate and the risks inherent in taking out a loan in a
foreign currency and asks national courts to check whether the seller or
supplier has communicated to the consumer all the relevant information
enabling him/her to assess their financial
obligations <a href="#ntr187-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0187"
id="ntc187-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0187">(<span
class="super note-tag">187</span>)</a>. It will also be relevant whether
the seller or supplier gave appropriate prominence to such important
information.

The Court has further stated that national courts, when assessing
compliance with transparency requirements, have to
<span class="italic">check</span> whether consumers received the
required information <a href="#ntr188-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0188"
id="ntc188-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0188">(<span
class="super note-tag">188</span>)</a> and have to take into account
also the <span class="bold">promotional material and information
provided by the lender in the negotiation</span> of the loan
agreement <a href="#ntr189-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0189"
id="ntc189-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0189">(<span
class="super note-tag">189</span>)</a>.

Where the nature of the contract term requires sellers or suppliers to
provide certain information or explanations prior to the conclusion of
the contract, they will also have to bear the burden to prove that they
provided consumers with the necessary information in order to be able to
claim that the relevant terms are plain and
intelligible <a href="#ntr190-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0190"
id="ntc190-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0190">(<span
class="super note-tag">190</span>)</a>.

While the rulings on transparency often relate to contract terms
defining the main subject matter of the contract or the remuneration or
contract terms that are closely related to those core aspects of the
contract, the transparency requirements under Article 5 are not limited
to the type of terms referred to in Article 4(2) UCTD. Transparency,
including predictability, is an important aspect, also in relation to
unilateral changes to the contract, in particular price changes, for
instance, in loan contracts or in long-term supply
contracts <a href="#ntr191-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0191"
id="ntc191-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0191">(<span
class="super note-tag">191</span>)</a>.

While all contract terms have to be in plain intelligible language, it
is likely that the extent of the pre-contractual information obligations
for sellers or suppliers stemming from the UCTD depends also on the
significance of the contract term for the transaction and its economic
impact.

The Court <a href="#ntr192-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0192"
id="ntc192-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0192">(<span
class="super note-tag">192</span>)</a> has been requested to give
guidance on the transparency criteria for the inclusion in a mortgage
loan contract of an index for the applicable interest rate established
by a national bank.

3.3.2.   <span class="italic">Transparency requirements stemming from
other EU acts</span>

Various EU acts regulate in a detailed fashion the pre-contractual
information that traders have to provide to consumers in general or with
regard to specific kinds of contracts. Examples include the Unfair
Commercial Practices
Directive <a href="#ntr193-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0193"
id="ntc193-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0193">(<span
class="super note-tag">193</span>)</a>, the Consumer Rights
Directive <a href="#ntr194-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0194"
id="ntc194-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0194">(<span
class="super note-tag">194</span>)</a>, the Consumer Credit
Directive <a href="#ntr195-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0195"
id="ntc195-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0195">(<span
class="super note-tag">195</span>)</a>, the Mortgage Credit
Directive <a href="#ntr196-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0196"
id="ntc196-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0196">(<span
class="super note-tag">196</span>)</a>, the Package Travel
Directive <a href="#ntr197-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0197"
id="ntc197-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0197">(<span
class="super note-tag">197</span>)</a>, the European Electronic
Communications Code <a href="#ntr198-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0198"
id="ntc198-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0198">(<span
class="super note-tag">198</span>)</a>, Regulation (EC) No 1008/2008 on
air services <a href="#ntr199-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0199"
id="ntc199-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0199">(<span
class="super note-tag">199</span>)</a> and Directives
2009/72/EC <a href="#ntr200-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0200"
id="ntc200-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0200">(<span
class="super note-tag">200</span>)</a> and
2009/73/EC <a href="#ntr201-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0201"
id="ntc201-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0201">(<span
class="super note-tag">201</span>)</a> concerning common rules for the
internal market in electricity natural gas. Such acts may also regulate
the compulsory content of the relevant
contracts <a href="#ntr202-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0202"
id="ntc202-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0202">(<span
class="super note-tag">202</span>)</a> and contain rules on the
admissibility of contract changes and their
transparency <a href="#ntr203-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0203"
id="ntc203-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0203">(<span
class="super note-tag">203</span>)</a>.

The UCTD is without prejudice to such provisions and the consequences of
the failure to comply with them as set out in such specific
instruments <a href="#ntr204-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0204"
id="ntc204-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0204">(<span
class="super note-tag">204</span>)</a>.

Insofar as specific pre-contractual and contractual information
requirements apply, they will also have to be taken into account for the
transparency requirements under the UCTD, on a case-by-case basis, and
in light of the purpose and scope of those instruments.

Thus, for instance, in relation to EU consumer credit
legislation <a href="#ntr205-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0205"
id="ntc205-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0205">(<span
class="super note-tag">205</span>)</a>, the Court has stressed the
importance of borrowers having to hand in all information which could
have a bearing on the extent of their
liability <a href="#ntr206-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0206"
id="ntc206-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0206">(<span
class="super note-tag">206</span>)</a> and, thereby, of presenting the
total cost of the credit in the form of a single mathematical
formula <a href="#ntr207-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0207"
id="ntc207-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0207">(<span
class="super note-tag">207</span>)</a>. Therefore, the failure to
indicate the annual percentage rate of charge (APR) as required under EU
consumer credit rules <a href="#ntr208-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0208"
id="ntc208-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0208">(<span
class="super note-tag">208</span>)</a> is ‘decisive evidence’ as to
whether the term of the agreement relating to the total cost of the
credit is drafted in plain intelligible language. This is true also
where the necessary information on the calculation of the APR is not
provided <a href="#ntr209-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0209"
id="ntc209-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0209">(<span
class="super note-tag">209</span>)</a>. The same must apply if the
indicated APR is erroneous or misleading. If the information on the
total cost of the loan required under EU consumer credit rules is not
provided or if the indication is misleading, the relevant terms will,
therefore, be deemed not to be plain and intelligible.

As regards mortgage credit contracts with consumers, all the rulings so
far handed down by the Court related to contracts concluded before the
entry into application of Directive
2014/17/EU <a href="#ntr210-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0210"
id="ntc210-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0210">(<span
class="super note-tag">210</span>)</a> on credit agreements for
consumers relating to residential property. For this reason, the Court
has not yet ruled on the relationship between specific information
requirements under Directive 2014/17/EU and the transparency
requirements under the UCTD. Directive 2014/17/EU imposes high
transparency standards by requiring clear and comprehensible general
information about credit agreements to be made available to consumers
through the European Standardized Information Sheet (ESIS) and the
calculation of the Annual Percentage Rate of Charge (APR). In relation
to foreign currency loans, Article 23(6) of Directive 2014/17/EU
requires that creditors and intermediaries disclose to the consumer, in
the ESIS and in the credit agreement, the arrangements available for
him/her to limit exposure to the exchange rate risk during the lifetime
of the credit. Where there is no provision in the credit agreement to
limit the exchange rate risk to which the consumer is exposed to a
fluctuation of less than 20 %, the ESIS shall include an illustrative
example of the impact of a 20 % fluctuation in the exchange rate.

The Court has applied <a href="#ntr211-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0211"
id="ntc211-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0211">(<span
class="super note-tag">211</span>)</a> transparency requirements
stemming from Directive
2003/55/EC <a href="#ntr212-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0212"
id="ntc212-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0212">(<span
class="super note-tag">212</span>)</a> concerning common rules for the
internal market in natural gas and the UCTD in a complementary fashion.

The fact of whether a seller or supplier has complied with
sector-specific requirements is an important element when assessing
compliance with the transparency requirements under the UCTD. However,
given the parallel applicability of the UCTD with sectorial legislation,
compliance with such instruments does not automatically indicate
compliance with all transparency requirements under the UCTD.
Furthermore, the fact that a specific act does not contain specific
information requirements does not exclude information obligations under
the UCTD on contract terms that sellers or suppliers add on their own
initiative.

3.4.   <span class="bold">Unfairness assessment under Articles 3 and
4(1) UCTD</span>

3.4.1.   <span class="italic">The framework for the assessment under
Articles 3(1) and 4(1)</span>

Contract terms are to be regarded as unfair under Article 3(1) if,

|     |                                             |
|-----|---------------------------------------------|
| —   | contrary to the requirements of good faith, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | they cause a significant imbalance in the parties' rights and obligations arising under the contract, to the detriment of the consumer. |

Although the Court has so far not been asked to explain the relationship
between those two criteria, the wording of Article 3(1) and of Recital
16 suggest that the absence of good faith is linked to the significant
imbalance in the rights and obligations created by a contract term.
Recital 16 refers to the bargaining power of the parties and explains
that the requirement of ‘good faith’ relates to the question of whether
a seller or supplier deals fairly and equitably with a consumer and
takes his legitimate interests into account. In this respect, the
Court <a href="#ntr213-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0213"
id="ntc213-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0213">(<span
class="super note-tag">213</span>)</a> finds it particularly relevant to
consider whether the seller or supplier could reasonably assume that the
consumer would have agreed to the term in individual negotiations:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘With regard to the question of the circumstances in which such an
imbalance arises “contrary to the requirement of good faith”, it should
be stated that, having regard to the 16th recital of Directive 93/13,
the national court must assess for those purposes whether the seller or
supplier, dealing fairly and equitably with the consumer, could
reasonably assume that the consumer would have agreed to such a term in
individual contract negotiations […] <a
href="#ntr214-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0214"
id="ntc214-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0214">(<span
class="super note-tag">214</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

This confirms that, for the purposes of Article 3(1), the concept of
good faith is an objective concept linked to the question of whether, in
light of its content, the contract term in question is compatible with
fair and equitable market practices that take the consumer's legitimate
interests sufficiently into account. It is, thereby, closely
linked <a href="#ntr215-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0215"
id="ntc215-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0215">(<span
class="super note-tag">215</span>)</a> to the (im)balance in the rights
and obligations of the parties.

The assessment of a <span class="bold">significant imbalance</span>
requires an examination as to how a contract term influences the rights
and obligations of the parties. Insofar as there are
<span class="italic">supplementary rules</span> from which the contract
term deviates, those will be the <span class="italic">primary
yardstick</span> for assessing a significant imbalance in the rights and
obligations of the parties <a href="#ntr216-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0216"
id="ntc216-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0216">(<span
class="super note-tag">216</span>)</a>. Where there are no relevant
statutory provisions, a significant imbalance will have to be assessed
in light <span class="italic">of other points of reference</span>, such
as fair and equitable market practices or a comparison of the rights and
obligations of the parties under a particular term, taking into account
the nature of the contract and other related contract terms.

Pursuant to Article 4(1) <a href="#ntr217-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0217"
id="ntc217-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0217">(<span
class="super note-tag">217</span>)</a>, the unfairness of a contract
term has to be assessed taking into account

|     |                                                                    |
|-----|--------------------------------------------------------------------|
| —   | the nature of the goods or services to which the contract relates, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | all the other terms of the contract or of another contract on which it is dependent, and |

|     |                                                                 |
|-----|-----------------------------------------------------------------|
| —   | all the circumstances attending the conclusion of the contract. |

The Member States may deviate from this general unfairness-test only for
the benefit of consumers, i.e. only if the national transposition makes
it easier to conclude that a contract term is
unfair <a href="#ntr218-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0218"
id="ntc218-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0218">(<span
class="super note-tag">218</span>)</a>.

The indicative list of contract terms in the
Annex <a href="#ntr219-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0219"
id="ntc219-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0219">(<span
class="super note-tag">219</span>)</a> to the UCTD is an essential
element on which the assessment as to whether a given term is unfair
under Article 3(1) may be
based <a href="#ntr220-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0220"
id="ntc220-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0220">(<span
class="super note-tag">220</span>)</a>. By contrast, where a given
contract term is covered by a national ‘black list’, there is no need to
carry out a case-by-case assessment based on the criteria of Article
3(1). A similar logic will apply where a Member State has adopted a list
of contract terms that are presumed to be unfair.

3.4.2.   <span class="italic">The relevance of statutory provisions and
the significance of the imbalance</span>

When assessing whether a contract term ‘causes a significant imbalance
in the parties’ rights and obligations, to the detriment of the
consumer', national courts have to carry out, in the first place, a
<span class="bold">comparison of the relevant contract term with any
rules of national law which would apply in the absence of the contract
term</span> <a href="#ntr221-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0221"
id="ntc221-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0221">(<span
class="super note-tag">221</span>)</a>, i.e. supplementary rules. Such
regulatory models can be found in particular in national contract law,
for instance in the rules setting out the consequences of a party's
failure to fulfil certain contractual obligations. This may include, the
conditions under which penalties, such as default interest, may be
requested or provisions on the statutory interest
rate <a href="#ntr222-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0222"
id="ntc222-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0222">(<span
class="super note-tag">222</span>)</a>.

Such comparative analysis will enable the national court to evaluate
whether and to what extent the contract term places the consumer in a
legal situation less favourable than under the otherwise applicable
contract law. The contract term may make the legal situation less
favourable for consumers for instance where it restricts the rights that
consumers would otherwise enjoy, or may add a constraint on their
exercise. It may also impose an additional obligation on the consumer
not envisaged by the relevant national
rules <a href="#ntr223-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0223"
id="ntc223-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0223">(<span
class="super note-tag">223</span>)</a>.

The imbalance in the rights and obligations to the detriment of the
consumer is <span class="bold">significant</span> if there is a
‘<span class="italic">sufficiently serious impairment of the legal
situation in which the consumer \[…\] is placed by reason of the
relevant national
provisions</span> <a href="#ntr224-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0224"
id="ntc224-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0224">(<span
class="super note-tag">224</span>)</a>’. This does not necessarily
require that the term must have a significant economic impact with
regard to the value of the
transaction <a href="#ntr225-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0225"
id="ntc225-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0225">(<span
class="super note-tag">225</span>)</a>. Therefore, for instance, a
contract term which imposes on the consumer payment of a tax where under
the applicable national legislation this tax should be borne by the
seller or supplier can create a significant imbalance in the parties'
rights and obligations, regardless of the amounts which the consumer
eventually will have to pay under such contract
term <a href="#ntr226-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0226"
id="ntc226-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0226">(<span
class="super note-tag">226</span>)</a>.

The effect of a contract term will also depend on its consequences under
the national legal system applicable to the contract, which means that
other legal provisions, including rules of procedure may have to be
taken into account as well <a href="#ntr227-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0227"
id="ntc227-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0227">(<span
class="super note-tag">227</span>)</a>. In this context, the difficulty
for the consumer to prevent the continued use of the type of contract
term in question may also be
relevant <a href="#ntr228-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0228"
id="ntc228-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0228">(<span
class="super note-tag">228</span>)</a>.

The Court has described the assessment of a significant imbalance in the
rights and obligations of the parties as
follows <a href="#ntr229-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0229"
id="ntc229-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0229">(<span
class="super note-tag">229</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘21</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>In that regard, the Court has held that in
order to ascertain whether a term causes a “significant imbalance” in
the parties' rights and obligations under a contract to the detriment of
the consumer, particular account must be taken of which rules of
national law would apply in the absence of an agreement by the parties
in that regard. Such a comparative analysis will enable the national
court to evaluate whether and, as the case may be, to what extent, the
contract places the consumer in a legal situation less favourable than
that provided for by the national law in force […] <a
href="#ntr230-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0230"
id="ntc230-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0230">(<span
class="super note-tag">230</span>)</a>.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>22</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>It thus appears that the question whether
that significant imbalance exists cannot be limited to a quantitative
economic evaluation based on a comparison between the total value of the
transaction which is the subject of the contract and the costs charged
to the consumer under that clause.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>23</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>On the contrary, a significant imbalance can
result solely from a sufficiently serious impairment of the legal
situation in which the consumer, as a party to the contract in question,
is placed by reason of the relevant national provisions, whether this be
in the form of a restriction of the rights which, in accordance with
those provisions, he enjoys under the contract, or a constraint on the
exercise of those rights, or the imposition on him of an additional
obligation not envisaged by the national rules.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>24</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>In that regard, the Court has confirmed
that, pursuant to Article 4(1) of the Directive, the unfairness of a
contractual term is to be assessed taking into account the nature of the
goods or services for which the contract in question was concluded and
by referring to all the circumstances attending its conclusion, as well
as all the other clauses in the contract […] <a
href="#ntr231-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0231"
id="ntc231-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0231">(<span
class="super note-tag">231</span>)</a>. In that respect, it follows that
the consequences of the term under the law applicable to the contract
must also be taken into account, requiring consideration to be given to
the national legal system […] <a
href="#ntr232-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0232"
id="ntc232-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0232">(<span
class="super note-tag">232</span>)</a>.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Where contractual arrangements infringe a statutory provision of
national or EU contract law from which the parties may not deviate by
way of contract, such contractual stipulations will generally be invalid
already directly by virtue of such provisions. Not individually
negotiated contract terms deviating from such provisions are likely to
violate also Article 3(1) UCTD.

3.4.3.   <span class="italic">Sanctions or consequences of the
consumer's failure to comply with contractual obligations</span>

In order not to cause a significant imbalance to the detriment of the
consumer, sanctions or consequences attached to the consumer's failure
to comply with contractual obligations have to be
<span class="bold">justified in light of the importance of the
consumer's obligation and the seriousness of the failure</span> to
comply with it <a href="#ntr233-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0233"
id="ntc233-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0233">(<span
class="super note-tag">233</span>)</a>. In other words, they have to be
proportionate <a href="#ntr234-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0234"
id="ntc234-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0234">(<span
class="super note-tag">234</span>)</a>. This assessment has to include
the question of whether the contract term derogates from statutory
provisions which would apply in the absence of a contract term on that
question and, where the term leads to a particular procedure, the
procedural means available to the
consumer <a href="#ntr235-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0235"
id="ntc235-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0235">(<span
class="super note-tag">235</span>)</a>.

The Court <a href="#ntr236-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0236"
id="ntc236-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0236">(<span
class="super note-tag">236</span>)</a> has presented the relevant
criteria with regard to so-called ‘acceleration’ or early repayment
clauses in mortgage credit agreements which allow the creditor to start
mortgage enforcement proceedings in the following way:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><p>‘[…] Article 3(1) and (3) of Directive 93/13 and Points 1(e) and
(g) and 2(a) of the annex thereto must be interpreted as meaning that,
in order to assess the unfairness of a contractual term accelerating the
repayment of a mortgage, […], the following are of decisive
importance:</p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>whether the right of the seller or supplier to
cancel the contract unilaterally is conditional upon the non-compliance
by the consumer with an obligation which is of essential importance in
the context of the contractual relationship in question,</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>whether that right is provided for in cases in
which such non-compliance is sufficiently serious in the light of the
contractual term and amount of the loan,</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>whether that right derogates from the rules
applicable in the absence of agreement between the parties, so as to
make it more difficult for the consumer, given the procedural means at
his disposal, to take legal action and exercise rights of the defence,
and</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>whether national law provides for adequate and
effective means enabling the consumer subject to such a contractual term
to remedy the effects of the unilateral cancellation of the loan
agreement.</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>It is for the referring court to carry out that assessment in
relation to all the circumstances of the particular case before
it.’</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

With regard to default interests, the
Court <a href="#ntr237-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0237"
id="ntc237-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0237">(<span
class="super note-tag">237</span>)</a> has explained this test as
follows:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…], regarding the term concerning the fixing of default interest,
it should be recalled that, in the light of paragraph 1(e) of the annex
to the Directive, read in conjunction with Articles 3(1) and 4(1) of the
directive, the national court must assess in particular, […], first, the
rules of national law which would apply to the relationship between the
parties, in the event of no agreement having been reached in the
contract in question or in other consumer contracts of that type and,
second, the rate of default interest laid down, compared with the
statutory interest rate, in order to determine whether it is appropriate
for securing the attainment of the objectives pursued by it in the
Member State concerned and does not go beyond what is necessary to
achieve them.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

In relation to the
proportionality <a href="#ntr238-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0238"
id="ntc238-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0238">(<span
class="super note-tag">238</span>)</a> and, thereby, unfairness of
sanctions set out in contract terms, the Court has furthermore
specified <a href="#ntr239-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0239"
id="ntc239-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0239">(<span
class="super note-tag">239</span>)</a> that it is necessary to evaluate
the <span class="italic">cumulative effect</span> of all the penalty
clauses in the contract in question, regardless of whether the creditor
actually insists that they all be satisfied in full.

Even if only the <span class="italic">cumulative effect</span> of the
sanctions makes them disproportionate, <span class="italic">all</span>
relevant contract terms have to be considered as
unfair <a href="#ntr240-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0240"
id="ntc240-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0240">(<span
class="super note-tag">240</span>)</a>, regardless of whether they have
been applied <a href="#ntr241-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0241"
id="ntc241-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0241">(<span
class="super note-tag">241</span>)</a>.

3.4.4.   <span class="italic">Possible unfairness of the price or
remuneration</span>

As mentioned above <a href="#ntr242-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0242"
id="ntc242-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0242">(<span
class="super note-tag">242</span>)</a>, under the minimum standard of
the UCTD, the adequacy of the price or remuneration is to be assessed
under Article 3(1) only if the contract terms determining the applicable
price or remuneration are not drafted in plain intelligible language.
For their assessment under Article 3(1), insofar as the relevant
national law does not contain supplementary rules, for instance, market
practices prevailing at the time when the contract was concluded, will
have to be taken into account when comparing the consideration to be
paid by the consumer and the value of a particular good or
service <a href="#ntr243-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0243"
id="ntc243-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0243">(<span
class="super note-tag">243</span>)</a>. For instance, regarding the
possible unfairness of an ordinary interest rate laid down in a loan
agreement, the Court has
stated <a href="#ntr244-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0244"
id="ntc244-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0244">(<span
class="super note-tag">244</span>)</a> that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘where the national court considers that a contractual term relating
to the calculation of ordinary interest, […], is not in plain
intelligible language, within the meaning of Article 4(2) of that
directive, it is required to examine whether that term is unfair within
the meaning of Article 3(1) of the directive. In the context of that
examination, it is the duty of the referring court, inter alia, to
compare the method of calculation of the rate of ordinary interest laid
down in that term and the actual sum resulting from that rate with the
methods of calculation generally used, the statutory interest rate and
the interest rates applied on the market at the date of conclusion of
the agreement at issue in the main proceedings for a loan of a
comparable sum and term to those of the loan agreement under
consideration;’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Taking into account also the ‘requirement of good faith’ in Article
3(1), the Commission considers that only fair and equitable market
practices can be considered for this assessment.

3.4.5.   <span class="italic">Circumstances at the time of the
conclusion of the contract</span>

According to Article 4(1), the unfairness of a contract term, i.e. the
significant imbalance against the requirements of good faith, has to be
assessed taking into account the nature of the contract, other contract
terms and other related contracts, as well as ‘all the circumstances
attending the conclusion of the contract’. The latter aspect does not
include circumstances manifesting themselves during the performance of
the contract. However, the circumstances attending the conclusion of the
contract must include all the circumstances which were known, or could
reasonably have been known, to the seller or supplier and which could
affect the future performance of the
contract <a href="#ntr245-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0245"
id="ntc245-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0245">(<span
class="super note-tag">245</span>)</a>.

One example for such circumstances is the risk of variations in the
exchange rate inherent to contracting a loan in a foreign currency,
which may materialise only during the performance of the contract. In
such cases it will be for national courts to assess, in light of the
knowledge and expertise of the lender, whether the consumer's exposure
to the exchange rate risk is in line with the requirements of good
faith, i.e. constitutes a fair and equitable practice and, gives rise to
a significant imbalance within the meaning of Article
3(1) <a href="#ntr246-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0246"
id="ntc246-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0246">(<span
class="super note-tag">246</span>)</a>.

Where contract terms are amended or replaced, it makes sense to take
into account the circumstances prevailing at the time of the amendment
or replacement when assessing the new contact
terms <a href="#ntr247-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0247"
id="ntc247-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0247">(<span
class="super note-tag">247</span>)</a>.

The significant imbalance has to be considered with regard to the
content of a contract term and <span class="italic">regardless of how it
has been applied in
practice</span> <a href="#ntr248-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0248"
id="ntc248-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0248">(<span
class="super note-tag">248</span>)</a>. For instance, where a contract
term allows a seller or supplier to demand immediate full repayment of
the loan if the consumer fails to pay a certain number of monthly
instalments, the unfairness has to be assessed based on the number of
unpaid monthly instalments required in the contract. It cannot be based
on the number of monthly instalments which the consumer had actually
failed to pay before the seller or supplier invoked the relevant
term <a href="#ntr249-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0249"
id="ntc249-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0249">(<span
class="super note-tag">249</span>)</a>.

3.4.6.   <span class="italic">Relevance of lack of transparency for the
unfairness of contract terms</span>

Lack of transparency does not automatically lead to the unfairness of a
given contract term under Article 3(1) of the
UCTD <a href="#ntr250-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0250"
id="ntc250-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0250">(<span
class="super note-tag">250</span>)</a>. This means that, after
establishing that a contract term covered by Article
4(2) <a href="#ntr251-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0251"
id="ntc251-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0251">(<span
class="super note-tag">251</span>)</a>‘is not in plain intelligible
language’, its unfairness normally still has to be assessed under the
criteria of Article 3(1) <a href="#ntr252-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0252"
id="ntc252-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0252">(<span
class="super note-tag">252</span>)</a>. Conversely, lack of transparency
is not an indispensable element in the assessment of unfairness under
Article 3(1) <a href="#ntr253-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0253"
id="ntc253-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0253">(<span
class="super note-tag">253</span>)</a> so that also contract terms that
are perfectly transparent can be unfair under Article 3(1) in light of
their unbalanced content <a href="#ntr254-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0254"
id="ntc254-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0254">(<span
class="super note-tag">254</span>)</a>.

However, insofar as contract terms are not plain and intelligible, i.e.
where sellers or suppliers do not comply with transparency requirements,
this circumstance can contribute to finding a contract term unfair under
Article 3(1) or can even indicate unfairness. Point 1(i) of the Annex,
in general, and Point 1(j) of the Annex, with particular regard to
unilateral changes to contract terms, confirm that lack of transparency
may be decisive for the unfairness of contract terms.

Several judgments refer to lack of transparency as a(n) (important)
element in the assessment of the unfairness at least of particular types
of contract terms <a href="#ntr255-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0255"
id="ntc255-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0255">(<span
class="super note-tag">255</span>)</a> or refer to the lack of
transparency and unfairness of contract terms in one
breath <a href="#ntr256-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0256"
id="ntc256-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0256">(<span
class="super note-tag">256</span>)</a>.

The Court has stressed the significance of transparency for the fairness
of contract terms, for instance, with regard to clauses which allow the
seller or supplier to change the rates to be paid by consumers in
long-term contracts <a href="#ntr257-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0257"
id="ntc257-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0257">(<span
class="super note-tag">257</span>)</a>, terms which determine the
consumer's core obligations in loan
agreements <a href="#ntr258-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0258"
id="ntc258-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0258">(<span
class="super note-tag">258</span>)</a> or with regard to choice-of-law
clauses <a href="#ntr259-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0259"
id="ntc259-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0259">(<span
class="super note-tag">259</span>)</a>.

The Court has indicated explicitly that, in relation to a choice-of-law
clause that fails to acknowledge the fact that, under the Rome I
Regulation consumers can always rely on the more advantageous rules of
their Member State of
residence <a href="#ntr260-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0260"
id="ntc260-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0260">(<span
class="super note-tag">260</span>)</a>, this omission of information or
the misleading character of the term can imply its unfairness. The
Court <a href="#ntr261-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0261"
id="ntc261-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0261">(<span
class="super note-tag">261</span>)</a>, after recalling the criterion of
a significant imbalance in the rights and obligations of the parties,
stated that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[i]n particular, the unfairness of such a term may result from a
formulation that does not comply with the requirement of being drafted
in plain and intelligible language set out in Article 5 of Directive
93/13. […]’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

One may thus conclude that, depending on the content of the contract
term at issue and in light of the impact of the lack of transparency,
<span class="bold">the possible unfairness of a contract term can be
closely related to its lack of transparency or the lack of transparency
of a contract term may even indicate its unfairness</span>. This may be
the case, for instance, where consumers cannot understand the
consequences of a term or are misled.

Indeed, where consumers are put in a disadvantageous position based on
contract terms which are unclear, hidden or misleading, or where
explanations necessary to understand their implications are not
provided, it is unlikely that the seller or supplier was dealing fairly
and equitably with the consumer and took their legitimate interests into
account.

3.4.7.   <span class="italic">Role of the Annex referred to in Article
3(3) UCTD</span>

As stated in Article 3(3) UCTD, the list in the Annex to the UCTD
contains ‘only’ an indicative and non-exhaustive list of the terms which
<span class="italic">may</span> be regarded as unfair. The Court has
stressed this on different
occasions <a href="#ntr262-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0262"
id="ntc262-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0262">(<span
class="super note-tag">262</span>)</a>. The non-exhaustive character of
the Annex and the minimum harmonisation principle under Article 8 UCTD
mean that national law may extend the list or use formulations leading
to stricter standards <a href="#ntr263-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0263"
id="ntc263-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0263">(<span
class="super note-tag">263</span>)</a>.

Since the list is only indicative, the terms contained therein should
not automatically be considered unfair. This means that their unfairness
still has to be assessed in light of the general criteria defined in
Articles 3(1) and 4 UCTD <a href="#ntr264-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0264"
id="ntc264-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0264">(<span
class="super note-tag">264</span>)</a>. The Court has specified that
terms listed in the Annex need not necessarily be considered unfair and,
conversely, terms not appearing in the list may none the less be
regarded as unfair <a href="#ntr265-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0265"
id="ntc265-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0265">(<span
class="super note-tag">265</span>)</a>. Nevertheless, the
<span class="italic">Annex is an important element in the assessment of
the unfairness of contract terms</span>. In the words of the Court,

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘if the content of the annex does not suffice in itself to establish
automatically the unfair nature of a contested term, it is nevertheless
an essential element on which the competent court may base its
assessment as to the unfair nature of that term <a
href="#ntr266-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0266"
id="ntc266-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0266">(<span
class="super note-tag">266</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Where a Member State <a href="#ntr267-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0267"
id="ntc267-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0267">(<span
class="super note-tag">267</span>)</a> has adopted a ‘black list’ of
terms that are always considered to be
unfair <a href="#ntr268-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0268"
id="ntc268-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0268">(<span
class="super note-tag">268</span>)</a>, contract terms that are
contained in such lists will not have to be assessed under the national
provisions transposing Article 3(1).

Otherwise, national authorities have to examine the term under Article
3(1), using the Annex as indication for what will normally constitute a
significant imbalance in the rights and obligations of the parties
contrary to the requirements of good faith.

In its case law, the Court has referred to the following points of the
Annex:

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | Point 1(e) <a href="#ntr269-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0269"
id="ntc269-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0269">(<span
class="super note-tag">269</span>)</a>: C-76/10 <span class="italic">Pohotovost'</span>; C-415/11 <span class="italic">Aziz</span> <a href="#ntr270-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0270"
id="ntc270-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0270">(<span
class="super note-tag">270</span>)</a>; Joint Cases C-94/17 and C-96/16 <span class="italic">Banco Santander Escobedo Cortés,</span> concerning late payment interests; |

</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

One of the merits of the Annex is that it can help to find a common
basis when Member States coordinate their enforcement actions in
relation to unfair contract terms. The Annex to the UCTD and the
different types of annexes in the national transpositions also make it
clearer to sellers or suppliers what kind of contract terms are
problematic, and can help enforcement bodies to enforce the UCTD in a
formal or informal manner.

4.   <span class="bold">NON-BINDING CHARACTER OF UNFAIR CONTRACT TERMS
(ARTICLE 6(1) UCTD)</span>

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><p><span class="italic">Article 6</span></p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>1.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Member States shall lay down that unfair
terms used in a contract concluded with a consumer by a seller or
supplier shall, as provided for under their national law, not be binding
on the consumer and that the contract shall continue to bind the parties
upon those terms if it is capable of continuing in existence without the
unfair terms.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>2.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Member States shall take the necessary
measures to ensure that the consumer does not lose the protection
granted by this Directive by virtue of the choice of the law of a
non-Member country as the law applicable to the contract if the latter
has a close connection with the territory of the Member
States</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><span class="italic">Recital 21</span></p>
<p>Whereas Member States should ensure that unfair terms are not used in
contracts concluded with consumers by a seller or supplier and that if,
nevertheless, such terms are so used, they will not bind the consumer,
and the contract will continue to bind the parties upon those terms if
it is capable of continuing in existence without the unfair
provisions;</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

4.1.   <span class="bold">The nature and role of Article 6(1) UCTD in
the protection against unfair contract terms</span>

The Court <a href="#ntr277-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0277"
id="ntc277-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0277">(<span
class="super note-tag">277</span>)</a> regularly emphasises the central
role of Article 6(1) in the system of protection of consumers under the
UCTD, which

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…]is based on the idea that the consumer is in a weak position
vis-à-vis the seller or supplier, as regards both his bargaining power
and his level of knowledge […] <a
href="#ntr278-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0278"
id="ntc278-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0278">(<span
class="super note-tag">278</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The non-binding character of unfair contract terms under Article 6(1) is
a <span class="italic"> <span class="bold">mandatory rule</span> </span>
through which the UCTD aims to tackle this inequality and create an
<span class="italic">effective
balance</span> <a href="#ntr279-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0279"
id="ntc279-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0279">(<span
class="super note-tag">279</span>)</a> between the parties under the
contract. In the words of the
Court <a href="#ntr280-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0280"
id="ntc280-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0280">(<span
class="super note-tag">280</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] Article 6(1) of the directive, according to which unfair terms
are not binding on the consumer, is a mandatory provision which aims to
replace the formal balance which the contract establishes between the
rights and obligations of the parties with an effective balance which
re-establishes equality between them.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Given that protection of consumers against unfair contract terms under
the UCTD is a matter of <span class="italic"> <span class="bold">public
interest</span> </span>, the
Court <a href="#ntr281-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0281"
id="ntc281-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0281">(<span
class="super note-tag">281</span>)</a> has repeatedly stated that
Article 6(1) is <span class="bold">of equal standing to the rules of
public policy laid down in the law of the Member States</span>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘The Court has, furthermore, held that, in view of the nature and
importance of the public interest underlying the protection which the
directive confers on consumers, Article 6 thereof must be regarded as a
provision of equal standing to national rules which rank, within the
domestic legal system, as rules of public policy […]. It must be held
that that classification extends to all the provisions of the directive
which are essential for the purpose of attaining the objective pursued
by Article 6 thereof.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The mandatory or imperative character of Article 6(1) means that this
provision is binding on all parties and authorities and cannot, in
principle, be deviated from. Article 6(2) UCTD confirms this by
specifying that consumers cannot lose their rights under the UCTD even
if the contract is governed by the law of a country other than a Member
State through an agreement on choice of the applicable
law <a href="#ntr282-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0282"
id="ntc282-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0282">(<span
class="super note-tag">282</span>)</a>.

The imperative character of Article 6(1) also implies that
<span class="bold">consumers cannot</span>, in principle,
<span class="bold">waive this protection</span>, neither by way of
contract <a href="#ntr283-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0283"
id="ntc283-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0283">(<span
class="super note-tag">283</span>)</a> nor by unilateral declaration,
whether directly or indirectly. This certainly applies prior to the
settlement of any dispute on specific claims related to the unfairness
of contract terms <a href="#ntr284-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0284"
id="ntc284-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0284">(<span
class="super note-tag">284</span>)</a>.

The substantive implications of Article 6(1) are presented in
sub-sections 4.2, 4.3 and 4.4 Procedural guarantees stemming from
Article 6(1) are discussed in Section 5. The substantive consequences
flowing from the unfairness of contract terms apply independently of
court proceedings and regardless of whether the unfairness of contract
terms is raised by the consumer or <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> by a court.

4.2.   <span class="bold">The legal effect of ‘not being binding on the
consumer’</span>

The notion of unfair contract terms not being binding on consumers can
be translated into different legal concepts at national level as long as
the protection sought by the UCTD is achieved. Nevertheless, the
<span class="italic">invalidity</span> of unfair contract terms would
appear to achieve the intended protection most effectively. The
Court <a href="#ntr285-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0285"
id="ntc285-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0285">(<span
class="super note-tag">285</span>)</a> has emphasised that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] Article 6(1) of Directive 93/13 must be interpreted as meaning
that a contractual term held to be unfair must be regarded, in
principle, <span class="bold">as never having existed</span>, so that it
<span class="bold">cannot have any effect on the consumer</span>.
Therefore, the determination by a court that such a term is unfair must,
in principle, have the consequence of restoring the consumer to the
legal and factual situation that he would have been in if that term had
not existed.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The non-binding character of unfair contract terms follows directly from
