the UCTD and does not require any prior declaration of unfairness or
invalidity of a contract term by a court or another authorised body.
However, such declarations provide legal certainty regarding the
(un-)fairness of a given contract term, in particular in cases where
there may be different views on its unfairness.

Therefore, the non-binding character cannot depend on whether or when a
consumer raised the unfairness of a given contract term or contested its
validity, as the Court <a href="#ntr286-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0286"
id="ntc286-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0286">(<span
class="super note-tag">286</span>)</a> has confirmed when stating that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] Article 6(1) of the Directive must be interpreted as meaning
that an unfair contract term is not binding on the consumer, and it is
not necessary, in that regard, for that consumer to have successfully
contested the validity of such a term beforehand.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

This also implies that consumers cannot be prevented, in principle, from
requesting a seller or supplier to remove a given unfair term from the
contract, from asking a national court that a contract term be declared
invalid or from objecting to claims from sellers or suppliers based on
unfair contract terms because of any applicable limitation
periods <a href="#ntr287-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0287"
id="ntc287-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0287">(<span
class="super note-tag">287</span>)</a>. The same applies to the power of
national courts to assess the unfairness of contract terms of their own
motion. The Court <a href="#ntr288-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0288"
id="ntc288-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0288">(<span
class="super note-tag">288</span>)</a> has stated that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] [t]he protection conferred on consumers by the Directive
precludes a national provision which, in proceedings brought by a seller
or supplier against a consumer on the basis of a contract concluded
between them, prohibits the national court, on expiry of a limitation
period, from finding, of its own motion or following a plea raised by
the consumer, that a term of the contract is unfair.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Where, in connection with an individual dispute or a collective action,
a national court finds a given term to be unfair, such finding or
declaration applies <span class="italic">ex tunc</span>. This means that
it must have effect from the conclusion of the contract or the moment
when the relevant term was inserted into the contract and
<span class="italic">not ex nunc</span> from the time of the
judgment <a href="#ntr289-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0289"
id="ntc289-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0289">(<span
class="super note-tag">289</span>)</a>.

4.3.   <span class="bold">Consequences of the unfairness of contract
terms for the rights and obligations of the parties</span>

This section discusses the principle that unfair contract terms have to
be set aside and may not be revised (Sub-section 4.3.1) and the specific
circumstances under which gaps in the contract caused by the elimination
of an unfair term may be filled (Sub-section 4.3.2).

4.3.1.   <span class="italic">The principle: Setting aside unfair
contract terms and prohibition from revising them</span>

Under Article 6(1), while unfair contract terms are not binding on
consumers, the remainder of the contract continues to bind the parties
‘if it is capable of continuing in existence without the unfair terms.’
The Court <a href="#ntr290-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0290"
id="ntc290-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0290">(<span
class="super note-tag">290</span>)</a> has stressed repeatedly that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…], in accordance with Article 6(1) of Directive 93/13, <span
class="bold">national courts are merely required to refrain from
applying an unfair contractual term</span> in order that it may not
produce binding effects with regard to the consumer, <span
class="bold">without being empowered to revise the content of that
term</span>. <span class="bold">That contract must continue in
existence</span>, in principle, <span class="bold">without any amendment
other than that resulting from the deletion of the unfair terms, in so
far as,</span> in accordance with the rules of domestic law, <span
class="bold">such continuity of the contract is legally possible</span>
[…] <a href="#ntr291-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0291"
id="ntc291-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0291">(<span
class="super note-tag">291</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

This means, for instance, with regard to unfair penalty clauses, that
national courts may not reduce the amount payable under the contract
term to an acceptable level, but simply have to discard the term in its
entirety <a href="#ntr292-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0292"
id="ntc292-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0292">(<span
class="super note-tag">292</span>)</a>.

The revision of unfair contract terms would, in fact, imply that the
terms in question would remain partially binding and that sellers or
suppliers would somewhat benefit from having used such terms. This would
undermine the effectiveness of Article 6(1) UCTD and remove the
dissuasive effect that Article 6(1) UCTD seeks to achieve by considering
unfair contract terms not to be
binding <a href="#ntr293-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0293"
id="ntc293-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0293">(<span
class="super note-tag">293</span>)</a>. The removal of such dissuasive
effect would also be inconsistent with the objective of combatting the
continued use of unfair contract terms reflected in Article 7(1)
UCTD <a href="#ntr294-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0294"
id="ntc294-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0294">(<span
class="super note-tag">294</span>)</a>.

Following the same logic, also the <span class="bold">partial
deletion</span> of an unfair contract term is inadmissible, as,
generally, it will be equivalent to a revision of a contract term by
altering its substance <a href="#ntr295-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0295"
id="ntc295-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0295">(<span
class="super note-tag">295</span>)</a>.

This may be different only in cases where what may look like one
‘contract term’ is, in reality, composed of different contract terms
within the meaning of Article 3(1). This may be the case in particular
where a contract term contains two (or more) stipulations that can be
separated from each other in such a way that one of them can be deleted,
while the remaining stipulations are still clear and comprehensible and
can be assessed on their own merits.

So far, the Court has given only a few indications regarding the
criteria for determining what constitutes a contract term in its own
right. It distinguishes, for instance, between contract terms setting
out the fundamental obligations for the consumer to pay back a loan in a
given currency and terms setting out the currency conversion
mechanism <a href="#ntr296-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0296"
id="ntc296-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0296">(<span
class="super note-tag">296</span>)</a>, which are therefore, by
definition, separate contract terms. The same applies to terms setting
out the price to be paid by the consumer and a mechanism for price
changes in long-term contractual
relations <a href="#ntr297-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0297"
id="ntc297-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0297">(<span
class="super note-tag">297</span>)</a>.

The Court <a href="#ntr298-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0298"
id="ntc298-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0298">(<span
class="super note-tag">298</span>)</a> has also distinguished between a
term determining the ordinary interest rate to be paid for a mortgage
loan and a term on late payment interest, even if the latter is defined
as a top-up to the ordinary interest rate. Having established that
ordinary interest and default interest have very different functions,
the Court explained that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] those considerations apply regardless of the way in which the
contractual term determining the default interest rate and that fixing
the ordinary rate of interest are worded. In particular, they apply not
only when the default interest rate is fixed independently of the
ordinary interest rate, in a separate contractual term, but also when
the default interest rate is fixed in the form of an increase in the
ordinary interest rate by a certain number of percentage points. In the
latter case, as the unfair term consists in that increase, Directive
93/13 requires solely that that increase be annulled.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

In connection with partial deletion, so far the Court has not indicated
whether the ‘blue pencil doctrine’, applied, for instance, by the German
Supreme Court, is compatible with the
UCTD <a href="#ntr299-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0299"
id="ntc299-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0299">(<span
class="super note-tag">299</span>)</a>. Under this doctrine, a
distinction is made between the inadmissible
revision <a href="#ntr300-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0300"
id="ntc300-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0300">(<span
class="super note-tag">300</span>)</a> of a contract term and the
permissible deletion of an unfair stipulation contained in a contract
term if the remaining content of the term can apply without any further
intervention. However, the Court has ruled that, in relation to a term
in a mortgage credit agreement which allowed the bank to call in the
entire loan after the consumer had failed to pay a single monthly
instalment, the early repayment obligation <span class="italic">cannot
be separated</span> from the condition of (only) one unpaid monthly
instalment <span class="italic">without altering the substance</span> of
those terms. In that case, the clause was thus not severable.

In sum,

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | what matters for the severability of contract terms is the content or function of particular stipulations rather than the way in which they are presented in a given contract and that |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | a partial deletion is not possible where two parts of a contract term are linked in such a way that the removal of one part would affect the substance of the remaining contract term. |

In this connection, it is, not excluded that a single paragraph/number
in a contract contains more than one contract term within the meaning of
Article 3(1) UCTD. Conversely, it is possible that two
paragraphs/numbers or even provisions in different documents form a
single contract term, in light of their content.

The principle that national courts may not revise unfair contract terms
applies regardless of whether unfairness is invoked by the consumer or
is considered <span class="italic">ex officio</span>.

However, this principle does not affect the right of the parties to
amend or replace an unfair contract term with a new one, within their
contractual freedom. If the new term is a contract term within the
meaning of Article 3(1) UCTD, it will have to be assessed on its own
merits pursuant to Articles 3, 4 and 5 UCTD. At the same time, the
amendment or replacement of an unfair contract term cannot, in
principle, remove the consumer's rights stemming from the non-binding
character of the amended/replaced term, such as restitution
claims <a href="#ntr301-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0301"
id="ntc301-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0301">(<span
class="super note-tag">301</span>)</a>. The Court may shed more light on
these questions in connection with so-called novation
agreements <a href="#ntr302-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0302"
id="ntc302-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0302">(<span
class="super note-tag">302</span>)</a>.

The principle that unfair contract terms simply have to be eliminated
from the contract, whereas the remainder of the contract continues to
bind the parties, does not raise difficulties in cases where the
contract can be performed without the unfair contract term(s). For
example, this can be the case for contractual penalties such as late
payment interests <a href="#ntr303-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0303"
id="ntc303-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0303">(<span
class="super note-tag">303</span>)</a>, clauses limiting the trader's
liability for improper performance, or choice-of-law, jurisdiction or
arbitration clauses. Cases where this is more complicated are discussed
in Section 4.3.2.

4.3.2.   <span class="italic">The exception: Filling gaps in the
contract to avoid its nullity</span>

According to Article 6(1) UCTD, the remainder of the contract will
continue to apply only if the contract ‘is capable of continuing in
existence without the unfair terms’.

Whether the continuity of the contract is possible without the unfair
term requires a ‘<span class="italic">legal assessment under the
applicable national
law</span> <a href="#ntr304-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0304"
id="ntc304-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0304">(<span
class="super note-tag">304</span>)</a>’. This implies a case-by-case
analysis as to whether the contract can, legally or technically, be
performed without the unfair contract term. Therefore, the assessment
cannot be based on purely economic considerations. The examination as to
whether the contract can continue in existence has to be
<span class="italic">objective</span>, i.e. it cannot be based on the
interests of only one party <a href="#ntr305-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0305"
id="ntc305-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0305">(<span
class="super note-tag">305</span>)</a>. This will entail that it should
not matter whether the seller or supplier would not have concluded the
contract without the unfair term or whether the deletion of the term
renders the contract less attractive from an economic point of view.

A contract cannot be performed, i.e. ‘cannot continue in existence’, if
a term defining its main subject matter or a term that is essential for
the calculation of the remuneration to be paid by the consumer is
removed <a href="#ntr306-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0306"
id="ntc306-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0306">(<span
class="super note-tag">306</span>)</a>. This applies, for instance, to
the designation of the currency in which payments have to be
made <a href="#ntr307-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0307"
id="ntc307-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0307">(<span
class="super note-tag">307</span>)</a> or to a term determining the
exchange rate in order to calculate the repayment instalments for a loan
denominated in a foreign
currency <a href="#ntr308-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0308"
id="ntc308-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0308">(<span
class="super note-tag">308</span>)</a>.

At the same time, account must be taken of the fact that Article 6(1)
aims to restore the balance between the parties through removing unfair
terms from the contract while, in principle, preserving the validity of
the contract as a whole, and not at the invalidity of all contracts
containing unfair terms <a href="#ntr309-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0309"
id="ntc309-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0309">(<span
class="super note-tag">309</span>)</a>. However, a Member State may
provide that a contract containing unfair terms may be declared invalid
as a whole where this ensures better consumer
protection <a href="#ntr310-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0310"
id="ntc310-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0310">(<span
class="super note-tag">310</span>)</a>.

The nullity of the contract may have negative consequences for the
consumer, for instance the obligation to pay back the whole loan
immediately rather than in the agreed instalments, which may run counter
to the protection intended by the UCTD. Therefore, the
Court <a href="#ntr311-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0311"
id="ntc311-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0311">(<span
class="super note-tag">311</span>)</a> has recognised that,
exceptionally, under certain conditions, national courts may replace an
unfair contract term with a supplementary provision of national law in
order to avoid the nullity of the contract. In relation to a case where
recourse to a supplementary provision avoided the nullity of a loan
agreement indexed in foreign currency caused by the unfairness of the
currency conversion mechanism, the Court stated:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘80</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>However, it does not follow, in a situation
such as that in the main proceedings, that Article 6(1) of Directive
93/13 precludes the national court, in accordance with the principles of
the law of contract, from deleting an unfair term and substituting for
it a supplementary provision of national law.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>81</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>On the contrary, replacing an unfair term
with such a provision which, as is clear from the thirteenth recital in
the preamble to Directive 93/13, is presumed not to contain unfair
terms, in that it leads to the result that the contract may continue in
existence in spite of the fact that Clause III/2 has been deleted and
continues to be binding for the parties, is fully justified in the light
of the purpose of Directive 93/13.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court further explained that ‘<span class="italic">the particularly
unfavourable consequences</span>’ of the annulment of the contract for
the consumer could jeopardise the intended dissuasive effect of the
elimination of the unfair contract
term <a href="#ntr312-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0312"
id="ntc312-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0312">(<span
class="super note-tag">312</span>)</a>.

Therefore, according to the case law to
date <a href="#ntr313-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0313"
id="ntc313-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0313">(<span
class="super note-tag">313</span>)</a>, before replacing unfair contract
terms with ‘supplementary rules of national law’ national courts have to
assess whether

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | <span class="italic">objectively</span> the elimination of an unfair contract term would otherwise lead to the nullity of the contract as a whole, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | <span class="italic">and</span> whether this has <span class="italic">particularly negative consequences</span> for the consumer <a href="#ntr314-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0314"
id="ntc314-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0314">(<span
class="super note-tag">314</span>)</a>, in light all relevant provisions of national law, including the rules of procedure <a href="#ntr315-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0315"
id="ntc315-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0315">(<span
class="super note-tag">315</span>)</a>. |

The UCTD neither defines the term ‘<span class="bold">supplementary
provision of national law</span>’ nor uses it. In a different context,
it refers to <span class="italic">‘rules which, according to the law,
shall apply between the contracting parties provided that no other
arrangements have been established’</span>. This quasi-definition
reflects what is generally understood to be the function of
supplementary provisions, and, when using this term in connection with
Article 6(1), the Court indeed refers to Recital 13 of the
UCTD <a href="#ntr316-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0316"
id="ntc316-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0316">(<span
class="super note-tag">316</span>)</a>.

The Court may further expand on the interpretation of the concept of
‘supplementary provisions of national law’. It may clarify, for
instance, whether it relates exclusively to provisions which
specifically regulate the rights and obligations of the parties to a
contract or whether it may also encompass general provisions of contract
law <a href="#ntr317-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0317"
id="ntc317-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0317">(<span
class="super note-tag">317</span>)</a>. Where such general provisions
allow for the creative adaption of the contract, the question arises as
to whether this is, in fact, equivalent to a non-admissible ‘revision’
of the relevant contract
term(s) <a href="#ntr318-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0318"
id="ntc318-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0318">(<span
class="super note-tag">318</span>)</a>.

The Court <a href="#ntr319-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0319"
id="ntc319-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0319">(<span
class="super note-tag">319</span>)</a> has indicated that,
<span class="italic">under specific circumstances</span>, statutory
provisions that serve as a model or reference for contract terms but
which are not technically supplementary provisions may be used to
replace an unfair contract term in order to prevent the nullity of the
contract.

The Court may also still clarify whether, under very specific
circumstances, other forms of filling the gap left by an unfair contract
term may be admissible <a href="#ntr320-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0320"
id="ntc320-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0320">(<span
class="super note-tag">320</span>)</a>.

When assessing the <span class="bold">particularly negative consequences
for consumers</span>, national courts have to take into account the
<span class="italic">interests of the consumer</span> at the time when
the question arises before the national
court <a href="#ntr321-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0321"
id="ntc321-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0321">(<span
class="super note-tag">321</span>)</a>. In cases where the continuation
of the contract is legally impossible following the elimination of an
unfair contract term <span class="italic">and where the continuation of
the contract would be contrary to the consumer's interests,</span> the
Court has specified that national courts may not preserve the validity
of the contract <a href="#ntr322-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0322"
id="ntc322-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0322">(<span
class="super note-tag">322</span>)</a>. In such cases, national law may
thus not prevent consumers from relying on the nullity of the contract
under Article 6(1) UCTD <a href="#ntr323-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0323"
id="ntc323-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0323">(<span
class="super note-tag">323</span>)</a>.

To date, the Court has not ruled
explicitly <a href="#ntr324-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0324"
id="ntc324-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0324">(<span
class="super note-tag">324</span>)</a> on whether the national court has
to establish the consumer's interest in the nullity of the contract
based exclusively on objective criteria or, rather, on the consumer's
preference as expressed within the proceedings. Nevertheless, there are
good arguments for respecting the consumer's preference, taking into
account that the consumer may even insist, in court proceedings, that an
unfair term is applied <a href="#ntr325-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0325"
id="ntc325-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0325">(<span
class="super note-tag">325</span>)</a>.

4.3.3.   <span class="italic">The application of supplementary
provisions in other cases</span>

So far, the Court has not ruled specifically on the question of whether
supplementary provisions of national law may be applied where the
deletion of a contract term does not lead to the nullity of the
contract, such as clauses on penalties, but where this does not imply a
‘revision’ of the unfair term by the national court. The
Court <a href="#ntr326-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0326"
id="ntc326-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0326">(<span
class="super note-tag">326</span>)</a> has ruled that the approach of a
national supreme court which did not apply any statutory default
interests after removing an unfair clause on late payment interests from
a contract was compatible with the UCTD. However, the Court has not
stated that this result was required by the UCTD. Nevertheless, the case
law discussed under point 4.3.2. may suggest that recourse to
supplementary provisions is possible only where the contract would
otherwise be void.

4.3.4.   <span class="italic">Possible application of unfair contract
terms despite their
unfairness?</span> <a href="#ntr327-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0327"
id="ntc327-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0327">(<span
class="super note-tag">327</span>)</a>

The Court <a href="#ntr328-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0328"
id="ntc328-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0328">(<span
class="super note-tag">328</span>)</a> has held that, in cases where the
contract can continue to exist without an unfair
term <a href="#ntr329-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0329"
id="ntc329-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0329">(<span
class="super note-tag">329</span>)</a>, and after the judge has informed
the consumer of the unfairness and non-binding nature of an unfair
contract term, the consumer may decide not to rely on this protection so
that in fact the contract term is applied.

4.4.   <span class="bold">Restitution of advantages obtained through
unfair contract terms</span>

Another consequence of the non-binding character of unfair contract
terms is that, where consumers have made payments based on unfair
contract terms, they must be entitled to the reimbursement of such
payments <a href="#ntr330-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0330"
id="ntc330-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0330">(<span
class="super note-tag">330</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘62</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>It follows that the obligation for the
national court to exclude an unfair contract term imposing the payment
of amounts that prove not to be due entails, in principle, a
corresponding restitutory effect in respect of those same
amounts.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>63</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>The absence of such restitutory effect would
be liable to call into question the dissuasive effect that Article 6(1)
of Directive 93/13, read in conjunction with Article 7(1) of that
directive, is designed to attach to a finding of unfairness in respect
of terms in contracts concluded between consumers and sellers or
suppliers.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Only provisions related to legal certainty, in particular
<span class="italic">res judicata</span> and reasonable limitation
periods, may limit such <span class="bold">restitutory
effect</span> <a href="#ntr331-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0331"
id="ntc331-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0331">(<span
class="super note-tag">331</span>)</a>. At the same time, Member States,
including national legislators and courts, may not limit in time the
effect of a finding that a given contract term is
unfair <a href="#ntr332-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0332"
id="ntc332-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0332">(<span
class="super note-tag">332</span>)</a> and thereby, for instance,
exclude restitution claims for the time prior to such
finding <a href="#ntr333-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0333"
id="ntc333-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0333">(<span
class="super note-tag">333</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘Article 6(1) of Council Directive 93/13/EEC […] must be interpreted
as precluding national case-law that temporally limits the restitutory
effects connected with a finding of unfairness by a court, in accordance
with Article 3(1) of that directive, in respect of a clause contained in
a contract concluded between a consumer and a seller or supplier, to
amounts overpaid under such a clause after the delivery of the decision
in which the finding of unfairness is made.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

In this connection, the Court recalled that it is for the Court alone,
in the light of the fundamental requirement of a general and uniform
application of EU law, to decide upon the temporal limitations to be
placed on the interpretation it lays down in respect of a rule of EU
law <a href="#ntr334-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0334"
id="ntc334-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0334">(<span
class="super note-tag">334</span>)</a>. In general, the interpretation
given by the Court of a rule of EU law must be applied by the national
courts also to legal relationships arising and established before the
Court's judgment since its interpretation establishes how the relevant
rule must be, or ought to have been, understood and applied from the
time of its entry into
force <a href="#ntr335-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0335"
id="ntc335-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0335">(<span
class="super note-tag">335</span>)</a>. Therefore, the Court may limit
the effect in time of its rulings only in ‘altogether exceptional cases’
in application of the general principle of legal certainty if two
cumulative conditions are met: (i) the market participants concerned
have acted in good faith and (ii) there is a risk of serious
difficulties due to the ‘retroactive’ application of the Court's case
law <a href="#ntr336-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0336"
id="ntc336-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0336">(<span
class="super note-tag">336</span>)</a>.

5.   <span class="bold">REMEDIES AND PROCEDURAL GUARANTEES REQUIRED BY
ARTICLES 6(1) AND 7(1) UCTD</span>

5.1.   <span class="bold">The significance of Articles 6(1) and 7(1)
UCTD and of the principles of equivalence and effectiveness in
general</span>

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><p><span class="italic">Article 6</span></p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>1.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Member States shall lay down that unfair
terms used in a contract concluded with a consumer by a seller or
supplier shall, as provided for under their national law, not be binding
on the consumer and that the contract shall continue to bind the parties
upon those terms if it is capable of continuing in existence without the
unfair terms.</span>
<p>[…]</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><span class="italic">Article 7</span></p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>1.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Member States shall ensure that, in the
interests of consumers and of competitors, adequate and effective means
exist to prevent the continued use of unfair terms in contracts
concluded with consumers by sellers or suppliers.</span>
<p>[…]</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><span class="italic">Article 47 of the Charter of Fundamental
Rights</span></p>
<p><span class="italic">Right to an effective remedy and to a fair
trial</span></p>
<p>Everyone whose rights and freedoms guaranteed by the law of the Union
are violated has the right to an effective remedy before a tribunal in
compliance with the conditions laid down in this Article.</p>
<p>[…]</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Articles 6(1) and 7(1) are the provisions of the UCTD laying down in
which way consumers are to be protected against unfair contract terms
and complement each other <a href="#ntr337-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0337"
id="ntc337-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0337">(<span
class="super note-tag">337</span>)</a>.

The implications of the non-binding nature of unfair contract terms for
the rights and obligations of the parties are presented in Section 4
above. This section discusses the implications of Article 6(1), in
conjunction with Article 7(1) and the principles of
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> and
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span>, for the rules of procedure
and the powers and obligations of national courts.

Article 7(1) UCTD reflects, with particular regard to unfair contract
terms, the general right to an effective remedy against the violation of
rights and freedoms guaranteed by EU law enshrined in Article 47 of the
Charter of Fundamental Rights of the
EU <a href="#ntr338-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0338"
id="ntc338-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0338">(<span
class="super note-tag">338</span>)</a>.

While Articles 6(1) and 7(1) do not contain any specific rules of
procedure, their objectives can be accomplished only if national rules
of procedure contribute to their achievement and do not raise
unjustified obstacles for consumers in order to rely on the protection
afforded to them by the UCTD.

In the absence of harmonisation of the rules of procedure in an
instrument of EU law, the Court has stressed the
<span class="italic">procedural autonomy</span> of the Member
States <a href="#ntr339-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0339"
id="ntc339-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0339">(<span
class="super note-tag">339</span>)</a>, but also their responsibility
for ensuring that rights deriving from EU law are effectively
protected <a href="#ntr340-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0340"
id="ntc340-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0340">(<span
class="super note-tag">340</span>)</a>. The Court has established that,
insofar as the Member States' rules of procedure affect the application
of rights laid down in EU law, such rules have to comply with the
principles of <span class="italic">equivalence</span> and
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> <a href="#ntr341-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0341"
id="ntc341-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0341">(<span
class="super note-tag">341</span>)</a>. It has referred to those
principles as the embodiment of the general obligation of the Member
States to ensure judicial protection of an individual's rights under EU
law <a href="#ntr342-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0342"
id="ntc342-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0342">(<span
class="super note-tag">342</span>)</a>.

<span class="italic"> <span class="bold">Equivalence</span> </span>
means that the procedural rules for safeguarding rights deriving from EU
law must not be less favourable than those applying to the protection of
similar rights under domestic
law <a href="#ntr343-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0343"
id="ntc343-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0343">(<span
class="super note-tag">343</span>)</a> or governing similar domestic
actions <a href="#ntr344-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0344"
id="ntc344-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0344">(<span
class="super note-tag">344</span>)</a>.

<span class="italic"> <span class="bold">Effectiveness</span> </span>
implies that the national rules of procedure may not make it virtually
or in practice <a href="#ntr345-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0345"
id="ntc345-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0345">(<span
class="super note-tag">345</span>)</a> impossible or excessively
difficult for citizens, including consumers, to exercise rights under EU
law <a href="#ntr346-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0346"
id="ntc346-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0346">(<span
class="super note-tag">346</span>)</a>.

The Court has described the meaning of
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> and
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> as
follows <a href="#ntr347-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0347"
id="ntc347-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0347">(<span
class="super note-tag">347</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘In the absence of Community rules governing the matter, it is for
the domestic legal system of each Member State to designate the courts
and tribunals having jurisdiction and to lay down the detailed
procedural rules governing actions for safeguarding rights which
individuals derive from the direct effect of Community law. However,
such rules must not be less favourable than those governing similar
domestic actions nor render virtually impossible or excessively
difficult the exercise of rights conferred by Community law […].’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court has developed these principles further with regard to consumer
law and especially the UCTD, drawing from them a number of specific
procedural requirements in order to ensure that consumers are
effectively protected against unfair contract terms also in the reality
of court proceedings.

Depending on the circumstances of the case and the questions raised by
the referring courts, the Court has based those requirements on:

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> <a href="#ntr348-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0348"
id="ntc348-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0348">(<span
class="super note-tag">348</span>)</a> of the non-binding nature of unfair contract terms under Article 6(1) UCTD, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the requirement of adequate and effective means to prevent the continued used of unfair contract terms under Article 7(1) UCTD <a href="#ntr349-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0349"
id="ntc349-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0349">(<span
class="super note-tag">349</span>)</a>, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the fundamental right to an effective remedy according to Article 47 of the Charter <a href="#ntr350-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0350"
id="ntc350-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0350">(<span
class="super note-tag">350</span>)</a>, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | as well as, depending on the applicable national law, the principle of <span class="italic">equivalence</span> <a href="#ntr351-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0351"
id="ntc351-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0351">(<span
class="super note-tag">351</span>)</a>. |

The Court refers to Article 7(1), sometimes supported by Article 47 of
the Charter, and <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> almost
interchangeably as a legal source for guarantees related to the
effectiveness of the procedural protection against unfair contract
terms <a href="#ntr352-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0352"
id="ntc352-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0352">(<span
class="super note-tag">352</span>)</a>.

The procedural requirements relate to the remedies and procedural rights
available to consumers, on the one hand, and duties of national courts,
on the other hand. Essentially they include the principles that

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | consumers must have effective remedies to raise the unfairness of relevant contract terms and |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | that national courts are obliged to assess the unfairness of contract terms of their own motion (<span class="italic">ex officio</span>). |

The Court has further developed these procedural guarantees in light of
specific types of procedure and procedural situations, such as ordinary
civil proceedings <a href="#ntr353-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0353"
id="ntc353-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0353">(<span
class="super note-tag">353</span>)</a>, appeal
procedures <a href="#ntr354-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0354"
id="ntc354-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0354">(<span
class="super note-tag">354</span>)</a>, judgments in
default <a href="#ntr355-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0355"
id="ntc355-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0355">(<span
class="super note-tag">355</span>)</a>, actions for the annulment of an
arbitration award <a href="#ntr356-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0356"
id="ntc356-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0356">(<span
class="super note-tag">356</span>)</a>, enforcement of an arbitration
award <a href="#ntr357-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0357"
id="ntc357-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0357">(<span
class="super note-tag">357</span>)</a>,
injunctions <a href="#ntr358-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0358"
id="ntc358-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0358">(<span
class="super note-tag">358</span>)</a>, different kinds of payment order
procedures <a href="#ntr359-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0359"
id="ntc359-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0359">(<span
class="super note-tag">359</span>)</a>, mortgage enforcement
procedures <a href="#ntr360-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0360"
id="ntc360-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0360">(<span
class="super note-tag">360</span>)</a>, voluntary
auctions <a href="#ntr361-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0361"
id="ntc361-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0361">(<span
class="super note-tag">361</span>)</a>, and insolvency
procedures <a href="#ntr362-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0362"
id="ntc362-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0362">(<span
class="super note-tag">362</span>)</a>. The Court has also been
requested to consider the relationship between Regulation (EC) No
1896/2006 <a href="#ntr363-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0363"
id="ntc363-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0363">(<span
class="super note-tag">363</span>)</a> creating a European order for
payment procedure and the procedural guarantees under the
UCTD <a href="#ntr364-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0364"
id="ntc364-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0364">(<span
class="super note-tag">364</span>)</a>.

While most preliminary rulings concerned cases where consumers were in
the position of the defendant or
debtor <a href="#ntr365-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0365"
id="ntc365-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0365">(<span
class="super note-tag">365</span>)</a>, the Court has applied these
principles also to proceedings where the consumer requested to declare a
contract term invalid.

Although the context and the specificities of each type of procedure
have to be taken into account when assessing the compatibility of
specific provisions with the UCTD, <span class="italic">
<span class="bold">the standards and tests developed by the Court apply
to all types of procedure.</span> </span>

The Court has emphasised
repeatedly <a href="#ntr366-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0366"
id="ntc366-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0366">(<span
class="super note-tag">366</span>)</a>, that procedures which give
creditors the possibility of a more expedient enforcement of their
claims based on titles other than judgments obtained in declaratory
proceedings and which entail no or only limited substantive checks by
national courts, must not deprive consumers of their right to proper
protection against unfair contract terms. This means that
<span class="bold">the specific type of procedure which a seller of
supplier chooses, or which otherwise applies, cannot reduce the
fundamental procedural guarantees required by the UCTD for the benefit
of consumers.</span> In the words of the
Court <a href="#ntr367-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0367"
id="ntc367-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0367">(<span
class="super note-tag">367</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] the specific characteristics of court proceedings cannot
constitute a factor which is liable to affect the legal protection from
which consumers must benefit under the provisions of Directive
93/13.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

At the same time, it is <span class="bold">necessary</span>, in
connection with the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span>
principle <a href="#ntr368-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0368"
id="ntc368-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0368">(<span
class="super note-tag">368</span>)</a>, <span class="bold">to look at
the national rules of procedure in their proper context and in their
entirety</span>. The Court <a href="#ntr369-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0369"
id="ntc369-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0369">(<span
class="super note-tag">369</span>)</a> has expressed this as follows:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘43</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>[…] with regard to the principle of
effectiveness, it should be noted that the Court has consistently held
that every case in which the question arises as to whether a national
procedural provision makes the application of EU law impossible or
excessively difficult must be analysed by reference to the role of that
provision in the procedure, its progress and its special features,
viewed as a whole, before the various national bodies […]</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>44</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>In that context, it is necessary to take
into consideration, where relevant, the principles which lie at the
basis of the national legal system, such as the protection of the rights
of the defence, the principle of legal certainty and the proper conduct
of the proceedings[…].’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

This means that account has to be taken of the protection which national
rules provide against unfair contract terms at different stages of the
procedure, for instance in the phase before the issuing of a payment
order and at the enforcement or opposition
stage <a href="#ntr370-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0370"
id="ntc370-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0370">(<span
class="super note-tag">370</span>)</a> or in connection with the
remedies against mortgage enforcement based on a notarial
deed <a href="#ntr371-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0371"
id="ntc371-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0371">(<span
class="super note-tag">371</span>)</a>.

National courts are obliged to apply these procedural guarantees also
where national provisions would otherwise prevent them from doing so,
and must disregard case law of national supreme courts insofar as it is
incompatible with the UCTD as interpreted by the
Court <a href="#ntr372-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0372"
id="ntc372-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0372">(<span
class="super note-tag">372</span>)</a>.

All procedural guarantees stemming from EU law apply to cases involving
unfair contract terms, even if they are not specifically mentioned in
this Notice. This includes the procedural rights mentioned in Article 47
of the Charter, including the right to a fair
hearing <a href="#ntr373-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0373"
id="ntc373-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0373">(<span
class="super note-tag">373</span>)</a> and the equality of
arms <a href="#ntr374-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0374"
id="ntc374-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0374">(<span
class="super note-tag">374</span>)</a>. The principle of effective
judicial protection of consumers does not, in itself, afford consumers a
right to a second level of
jurisdiction <a href="#ntr375-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0375"
id="ntc375-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0375">(<span
class="super note-tag">375</span>)</a> for the assessment of contract
terms. However, such right may be based on Article 7(1) UCTD in
conjunction with the principle of equality of arms, guaranteed under
Article 47 of the Charter, where, in the same proceedings, sellers or
suppliers have the right to appeal a decision regarding the unfairness
of contract terms <a href="#ntr376-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0376"
id="ntc376-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0376">(<span
class="super note-tag">376</span>)</a>.

5.2.   <span class="bold">The principle of <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> control of unfair contract
terms</span> <a href="#ntr377-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0377"
id="ntc377-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0377">(<span
class="super note-tag">377</span>)</a>

5.2.1.   <span class="italic">Link to Articles 6(1) and 7(1)</span>

In order to compensate for the structurally weaker position of
consumers, who may not be aware of their rights and may, therefore, not
raise the unfairness of contract terms, national courts, as a neutral
instance, play an active role in proceedings involving unfair contract
terms. Since its ruling of 4 June
2009 <a href="#ntr378-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0378"
id="ntc378-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0378">(<span
class="super note-tag">378</span>)</a>, the Court has consistently held
that <span class="bold">national courts are under an
<span class="italic">obligation</span> to assess unfair contract terms
of their own motion</span> (<span class="italic">ex officio</span>),
i.e. even if the unfairness of contract terms is not raised by the
consumer:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘1.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Article 6(1) of Council Directive 93/13/EEC
of 5 April 1993, on unfair terms in consumer contracts, must be
interpreted as meaning that an unfair contract term is not binding on
the consumer, and it is not necessary, in that regard, for that consumer
to have successfully contested the validity of such a term
beforehand.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>2.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>The national court is required to examine,
of its own motion, the unfairness of a contractual term where it has
available to it the legal and factual elements necessary for that task.
Where it considers such a term to be unfair, it must not apply it,
except if the consumer opposes that non-application. That duty is also
incumbent on the national court when it is ascertaining its own
territorial jurisdiction.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court has repeatedly confirmed this
requirement <a href="#ntr379-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0379"
id="ntc379-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0379">(<span
class="super note-tag">379</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] the Court has stated on several occasions that the national
court is required to assess of its own motion whether a contractual term
falling within the scope of Directive 93/13 is unfair, compensating in
this way for the imbalance which exists between the consumer and the
seller or supplier, where it has available to it the legal and factual
elements necessary for that task […] <a
href="#ntr380-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0380"
id="ntc380-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0380">(<span
class="super note-tag">380</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<span class="italic">Ex officio</span> control aims to achieve the
result sought by Article 6(1) in individual cases and contributes to the
objective of Article 7 as it may act as a deterrent to the use of unfair
contract terms at large <a href="#ntr381-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0381"
id="ntc381-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0381">(<span
class="super note-tag">381</span>)</a>. The obligation of
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> control applies
<span class="italic">a fortiori</span> where a consumer, in substance,
challenges the validity or fairness of the contract without, however,
referring specifically to the legal provisions on unfair contract
terms <a href="#ntr382-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0382"
id="ntc382-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0382">(<span
class="super note-tag">382</span>)</a>.

5.2.2.   <span class="italic">Relationship with principles of civil
procedure</span>

Across the Member States, the guiding principle in civil
proceedings <a href="#ntr383-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0383"
id="ntc383-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0383">(<span
class="super note-tag">383</span>)</a> is party disposition (or party
autonomy). This usually implies that it is in the sole discretion of the
parties to define the subject matter and extent of the proceedings so
that the judge cannot grant a claim that has not been made
(<span class="italic">ultra petita</span>) or to grant more than was
claimed (<span class="italic">extra petita</span>). It is also widely
accepted that it is primarily the responsibility of the parties to
present the facts relied on in order to substantiate their requests as
well as to submit the necessary evidence. In general, each party will
bear the burden of proof for the facts supporting their submission,
unless there are specific provisions shifting or alleviating the burden
of proof for certain questions.

It is generally recognised that, while the parties have to provide the
facts, it is for the court to make the necessary legal
qualifications <a href="#ntr384-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0384"
id="ntc384-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0384">(<span
class="super note-tag">384</span>)</a>, which is expressed in the
principles <span class="italic">da mihi factum dabo tibi jus</span> and
<span class="italic">iura novit curia.</span> It is also normal that
courts have to consider certain imperative rules, often referred to as
matters of public policy, of their own motion, i.e. without having to be
prompted by the parties.

Within this general framework, there are differences between the Member
States regarding the extent to which the courts may or are obliged to
adopt a more active role in the
proceedings <a href="#ntr385-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0385"
id="ntc385-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0385">(<span
class="super note-tag">385</span>)</a>, including a more inquisitorial
or investigative role, for instance by asking questions, giving hints or
feedback, but also regarding the taking of evidence.

<span class="italic">Ex officio</span> control of the unfairness of
contract terms is fundamentally the procedural consequence of the fact
that the unfairness of contract terms and their non-binding nature are
mandatory rules of public policy which apply <span class="italic">ex
jure</span> and which are legal aspects that, therefore, do not depend
on any party invoking it. <span class="italic">Ex officio</span> control
of unfair contract terms is, therefore, not in conflict with the
fundamental principles of civil proceedings such as party disposition.
Nevertheless, specific national provisions may make
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> control difficult or impossible.
For further details on such instances, see Sections 5.4, 5.5 and 5.6.

5.2.3.   <span class="italic">Ex officio control and total passivity on
the part of the consumer</span>

Consumers are generally expected to use available remedies and not to
stay completely passive in order to benefit from protection under the
UCTD. The Court has acknowledged that the
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> principle cannot be stretched
so far as to require a national court to make up fully for a consumer's
total inertia <a href="#ntr386-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0386"
id="ntc386-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0386">(<span
class="super note-tag">386</span>)</a> in cases where they can bring
effective remedies under reasonable
conditions <a href="#ntr387-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0387"
id="ntc387-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0387">(<span
class="super note-tag">387</span>)</a>. Accordingly, the mere fact that
a consumer may have to bring court proceedings and use remedies in order
to obtain protection against unfair contract terms is
<span class="italic">not automatically</span> contrary to the principle
of effectiveness <a href="#ntr388-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0388"
id="ntc388-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0388">(<span
class="super note-tag">388</span>)</a>. At the same time, the Court's
case law implies that national courts have to assess the unfairness of
contract terms of their own motion even where consumers stayed
completely passive, where such intervention is required by the
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> principle, as discussed in
Section 5.3, or by Article 7(1) or the effectiveness principle, as
discussed in Section 5.4.

5.3.   <span class="bold">Obligations stemming from the principle of
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> </span>

5.3.1.   <span class="italic">Ex officio control of unfair contract
terms</span>

Under the principle of
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> <a href="#ntr389-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0389"
id="ntc389-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0389">(<span
class="super note-tag">389</span>)</a>, national courts or tribunals are
obliged to consider binding rules of EU law of their own motion in all
cases where domestic law obliges them or gives them at least the power
or discretion to raise points of law based on binding domestic rules of
their own motion. As stated above, the non-binding character of unfair
contract terms set out in Article 6(1) and all the provisions of the
UCTD essential for attaining this objective must be treated as
<span class="italic">equivalent</span> to public policy considerations
recognised under the law of the Member States. This status will apply to
all provisions of the UCTD that are relevant when assessing the
unfairness of a contract term and drawing the consequences from this
finding.

The Court <a href="#ntr390-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0390"
id="ntc390-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0390">(<span
class="super note-tag">390</span>)</a> has explained this in the
following way,

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘44</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>[…], in view of the nature and importance of
the public interest underlying the protection which the directive
confers on consumers, Article 6 thereof must be regarded as a provision
of equal standing to national rules which rank, within the domestic
legal system, as rules of public policy[…]. It must be held that that
classification extends to all the provisions of the directive which are
essential for the purpose of attaining the objective pursued by Article
6 thereof.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>45</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>It follows that, where the national court
has the power, under internal procedural rules, to examine of its own
motion the validity of a legal measure in the light of national rules of
public policy, […], it must also exercise that power for the purposes of
assessing of its own motion, in the light of the criteria laid down in
the directive, whether a contractual term coming within the scope of
that directive may be unfair.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>46</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>The national court is also under such an
obligation where, under the domestic legal system, it merely has a
discretion to consider of its own motion whether such a term is in
conflict with national rules of public policy […].’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Therefore, national courts have to assess the unfairness of relevant
contract terms of their own motion whenever national law obliges them or
gives them the possibility to check <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> compliance with <span class="italic">any public policy
considerations</span> mentioned in the relevant national provisions,
including, for instance, legal prohibitions, basic
morality <a href="#ntr391-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0391"
id="ntc391-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0391">(<span
class="super note-tag">391</span>)</a>, or public policy in
general <a href="#ntr392-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0392"
id="ntc392-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0392">(<span
class="super note-tag">392</span>)</a>. In this respect, the
Court <a href="#ntr393-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0393"
id="ntc393-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0393">(<span
class="super note-tag">393</span>)</a> has stated, for instance, that,

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘where the court seised with a view to the enforcement of an
arbitration award may, of its own motion, discontinue the application of
that arbitration award where that award imposes on the party concerned
an objectively impossible payment, prohibited by law or contrary to
basic morality, that court must, where it has available to it the legal
and factual elements necessary for that task, examine, of its own
motion, within the context of the enforcement proceedings, also whether
a penalty <a href="#ntr394-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0394"
id="ntc394-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0394">(<span
class="super note-tag">394</span>)</a> laid down by a credit contract or
an arbitration clause <a href="#ntr395-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0395"
id="ntc395-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0395">(<span
class="super note-tag">395</span>)</a> is unfair.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The obligation of <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control based
on the <span class="italic">equivalence</span> principle applies to all
types and stages of the procedure, including judgments in
default <a href="#ntr396-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0396"
id="ntc396-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0396">(<span
class="super note-tag">396</span>)</a>, appeal
proceedings <a href="#ntr397-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0397"
id="ntc397-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0397">(<span
class="super note-tag">397</span>)</a>, or enforcement
proceedings <a href="#ntr398-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0398"
id="ntc398-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0398">(<span
class="super note-tag">398</span>)</a> whenever national law empowers
national judges to examine compliance with rules of public policy.

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><p>Therefore, <span class="bold">national courts are obliged to
apply the relevant national provisions on <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> control <span class="italic">mutatis mutandis</span> in
order to assess the unfairness of contract terms of their own
motion</span> <a href="#ntr399-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0399"
id="ntc399-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0399">(<span
class="super note-tag">399</span>)</a>.</p>
<p>Unlike under the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> principle,
this obligation is independent of any further assessment as to whether,
without such <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control, there is no
effective protection against unfair contract terms.</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

5.3.2.   <span class="italic">Other obligations based on the principle
of equivalence</span>

The principle of <span class="italic">equivalence</span> applies
likewise to other procedural rules. For instance, the
Court <a href="#ntr400-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0400"
id="ntc400-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0400">(<span
class="super note-tag">400</span>)</a> has ruled that less advantageous
rules for the intervention of consumer associations in relation to an
objection to a payment order based on the unfairness of contract terms
as compared to the rules applying to disputes concerning exclusively
national law would infringe the <span class="italic">equivalence</span>
principle.

The same must apply to any time-limits, rights to be heard, conditions
for interim measures, rights to object or appeal and indeed all other
procedural arrangements.

5.4.   <span class="bold"> <span class="italic">Ex officio</span>
assessment and the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> of
remedies</span>

5.4.1.   <span class="italic">The applicable test</span>

Under Article 7(1) UCTD or the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span>
principle <a href="#ntr401-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0401"
id="ntc401-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0401">(<span
class="super note-tag">401</span>)</a>, national law has to provide
remedies which allow consumers to invoke the unfairness of contract
terms, and those remedies have to be effective. This implies that
consumers must be able to bring such remedies <span class="italic">under
reasonable conditions</span>, meaning that there must not be
requirements or limitations which make it
<span class="italic">practically impossible or excessively
difficult</span> for them to obtain the required protection. Moreover,
consumers may be prevented from using legal remedies not only by
procedural obstacles, but also because of their
<span class="italic">limited knowledge or information</span>.

Therefore, in order to establish whether there are effective remedies,
the Court <a href="#ntr402-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0402"
id="ntc402-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0402">(<span
class="super note-tag">402</span>)</a> applies the
<span class="bold">overarching test</span> as to whether there is
<span class="bold">a significant risk that consumers will not benefit
from effective protection</span>

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | either because specific procedural requirements or limitations make it excessively difficult (or even practically impossible) to bring any available remedies, |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | <span class="italic"> <span class="bold">or</span> </span>, alternatively, because consumers do not have the necessary knowledge of their rights or do not receive the necessary information in order to use the remedies effectively. |

This test is reflected in several rulings, for instance, with regard to
payment order proceedings <a href="#ntr403-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0403"
id="ntc403-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0403">(<span
class="super note-tag">403</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘There is a <span class="bold">significant risk</span> that the
consumers concerned will not lodge the objection required, be it because
of the particularly short period prescribed for that purpose, or because
they might be dissuaded from defending themselves in view of the costs
which legal proceedings would entail in relation to the amount of the
disputed debt, <span class="bold">or</span> because they are unaware of
or do not appreciate the extent of their rights, or indeed because of
the limited content of the application for the order for payment lodged
by the seller or supplier, and thus the incomplete nature of the
information available to them […].’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

As explained in Section 5.1, it is, in connection with
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span>, necessary to consider the
relevant rules of procedure in their entirety, taking into account its
different stages <a href="#ntr404-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0404"
id="ntc404-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0404">(<span
class="super note-tag">404</span>)</a>. Relevant factors for the
assessment of effectiveness are discussed in Section 5.4.2 below.

Where there is a significant risk that consumers may not object to a
payment order, the Court established that <span class="bold">national
courts must assess the unfairness of contract terms of their own motion
at some stage of the procedure and at the latest before the enforcement
is carried out against a
consumer</span> <a href="#ntr405-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0405"
id="ntc405-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0405">(<span
class="super note-tag">405</span>)</a>. In the words of the
Court <a href="#ntr406-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0406"
id="ntc406-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0406">(<span
class="super note-tag">406</span>)</a>,

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘Indeed, effective protection of the rights conferred on the consumer
by Directive 93/13 can be guaranteed only if the national procedural
system allows the court, during the order for payment proceedings or the
enforcement proceedings concerning an order for payment, to check of its
own motion whether terms of the contract concerned are unfair […].’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

That means that,

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | where there is a significant risk that the consumer will not use remedies against a payment order, the court is obliged to assess the unfairness of relevant contract terms of its own motion <span class="italic">before</span> issuing the payment order <a href="#ntr407-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0407"
id="ntc407-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0407">(<span
class="super note-tag">407</span>)</a>. |

On the other hand,

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | where <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control did not take place before the order is granted, it has to be carried out, as a last resort, at the enforcement stage <a href="#ntr408-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0408"
id="ntc408-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0408">(<span
class="super note-tag">408</span>)</a>. |

Similarly,

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | if the checks carried out at an earlier stage of the procedure did not cover all relevant contract terms, national courts are obliged to assess other relevant contract terms including of their own motion even if the earlier checks were completed with a decision that has the effect of <span class="italic">res judicata</span> under the national rules of procedure <a href="#ntr409-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0409"
id="ntc409-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0409">(<span
class="super note-tag">409</span>)</a>. |

The Court <a href="#ntr410-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0410"
id="ntc410-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0410">(<span
class="super note-tag">410</span>)</a>, has also specified that the fact
that the unfairness of contract terms is assessed by a
<span class="italic">court official below the status of
magistrate</span> before a payment order is issued does not provide the
required protection. This means that, if there is a significant risk
that a consumer will not lodge an objection, a
<span class="italic">judge</span> still has to assess the unfairness of
contract terms, if necessary of their own motion, and, at the latest, at
the stage of enforcement.

Regarding specifically mortgage enforcement, the
Court <a href="#ntr411-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0411"
id="ntc411-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0411">(<span
class="super note-tag">411</span>)</a> considered it acceptable, in
principle, that enforcement proceedings can be initiated based on a
notarial deed without <span class="italic">prior judicial ex
officio</span> control of unfair contract terms. However, this is
compatible with the UCTD only insofar as consumers can take legal action
against such enforcement under reasonable conditions, including the
availability of interim measures, <span class="italic">and</span> if
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> control of the unfairness of
contract terms is guaranteed in the ensuing declaratory proceedings.

Therefore, mortgage enforcement based on a notarial deed is not
compatible with the UCTD if no effective remedies are available to
consumers or if there is a significant risk that consumers will not use
them. There are no effective remedies, for instance, where consumers
cannot object to the enforcement based on the unfairness of contract
terms in the enforcement proceedings, whereas in declaratory proceedings
in which the unfairness of contract terms can be assessed they cannot
obtain the suspension of the enforcement
proceedings <a href="#ntr412-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0412"
id="ntc412-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0412">(<span
class="super note-tag">412</span>)</a>.

The logic of these principles must apply <span class="italic">mutatis
mutandis</span> to all types of
procedure <a href="#ntr413-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0413"
id="ntc413-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0413">(<span
class="super note-tag">413</span>)</a>.

5.4.2.   <span class="italic">Relevant factors for the effectiveness of
remedies</span>

When assessing the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> of remedies
the specificities of the procedure in question will have to be taken
into account. Furthermore, the impact that particular obstacles may have
on the ability of consumers to bring remedies or the effect that limited
knowledge and information may have in this respect, should take into
account the perspective of more vulnerable consumers. Such consumers may
be particularly reluctant to use available remedies even where the
contract terms used against them are clearly
unfair <a href="#ntr414-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0414"
id="ntc414-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0414">(<span
class="super note-tag">414</span>)</a>.

The following <span class="bold">factors</span> are
<span class="bold">alternative</span>. This means that ineffectiveness
of the remedy can be caused either by a single requirement, for instance
high or discriminatory court
fees <a href="#ntr415-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0415"
id="ntc415-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0415">(<span
class="super note-tag">415</span>)</a>, or by a combination of different
requirements, e.g. a short time-limit combined with the need to take a
lawyer <a href="#ntr416-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0416"
id="ntc416-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0416">(<span
class="super note-tag">416</span>)</a> or the need to make detailed
submissions <a href="#ntr417-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0417"
id="ntc417-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0417">(<span
class="super note-tag">417</span>)</a>. While most of the aspects
referred to below concern procedural law, it is immaterial, in this
context, whether a given factor is qualified as a matter of procedural
or substantive law <a href="#ntr418-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0418"
id="ntc418-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0418">(<span
class="super note-tag">418</span>)</a> in the relevant Member State. The
list below is not exhaustive but reflects the most common examples
stemming from the case law of the Court.

<span class="expanded">Rules on jurisdiction</span>

The Court has stated that the right to an effective remedy applies to
the rules of jurisdiction as well as to the rules of
procedure <a href="#ntr419-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0419"
id="ntc419-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0419">(<span
class="super note-tag">419</span>)</a>. While Regulation (EU) No
1215/2012 <a href="#ntr420-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0420"
id="ntc420-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0420">(<span
class="super note-tag">420</span>)</a> on jurisdiction and the
recognition and enforcement of judgments in civil and commercial matters
contains protective rules for consumers in relation to cross-border
proceedings <a href="#ntr421-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0421"
id="ntc421-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0421">(<span
class="super note-tag">421</span>)</a>, a similar protection may not
exist under national rules on jurisdiction in domestic cases.
Jurisdiction rules, directly or
indirectly <a href="#ntr422-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0422"
id="ntc422-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0422">(<span
class="super note-tag">422</span>)</a>, obliging consumers to bring
their cases or defend themselves in courts that are some distance away
from their place of residence may discourage consumers from using
remedies, in particular insofar as physical presence is required in the
proceedings at issue <a href="#ntr423-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0423"
id="ntc423-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0423">(<span
class="super note-tag">423</span>)</a>. Here national courts have to
examine whether the distance to the court generates overly high travel
costs for the consumer such as to deter him/her from entering an
appearance in the proceedings brought against
him/her <a href="#ntr424-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0424"
id="ntc424-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0424">(<span
class="super note-tag">424</span>)</a>.

However, the fact that a certain case has to be heard not by the local
court, but by a higher level court, which is further away and may
require higher fees does not automatically imply an infringement of
Article 7(1) UCTD <a href="#ntr425-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0425"
id="ntc425-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0425">(<span
class="super note-tag">425</span>)</a>. Furthermore, consumer
organisations bringing collective proceedings are not in the same
position as individual consumers regarding jurisdiction
rules <a href="#ntr426-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0426"
id="ntc426-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0426">(<span
class="super note-tag">426</span>)</a>.

<span class="expanded">Time limits</span>

According to settled case law, it is compatible with EU law to lay down
‘reasonable’ time-limits for bringing proceedings in the interests of
legal certainty <a href="#ntr427-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0427"
id="ntc427-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0427">(<span
class="super note-tag">427</span>)</a>. Reasonable time-limits are not
in themselves liable to make it virtually impossible or excessively
difficult to exercise rights conferred by EU
law <a href="#ntr428-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0428"
id="ntc428-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0428">(<span
class="super note-tag">428</span>)</a>.

Short time-limits can be problematic already because of the little time
they give to consumers to consider their options, which may often
involve a legal assessment, including the need to seek legal advice. So
far, the Court has considered the length of time-limits on a
case-by-case basis and mainly in conjunction with other circumstances,
so that there is <span class="italic">no absolute scale as to what
time-limits are reasonable and which ones are not</span>.

Thus, the Court has considered a two-month time-limit to challenge an
arbitration award following its notification to be
reasonable <a href="#ntr429-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0429"
id="ntc429-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0429">(<span
class="super note-tag">429</span>)</a>. In contrast,
it <a href="#ntr430-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0430"
id="ntc430-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0430">(<span
class="super note-tag">430</span>)</a> considered a 20-day time-limit to
object to a payment order as ‘particularly short’, but also took into
account the obligation to be represented by a lawyer and the associated
fees, which may dissuade consumers from defending themselves.

In relation to extrajudicial enforcement of a
lien <a href="#ntr431-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0431"
id="ntc431-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0431">(<span
class="super note-tag">431</span>)</a>, the Court took into account that
a sale by auction could be contested within 30 days of the notice of
enforcement of the charge and that consumers had a period of three
months following the public auction to take steps. Furthermore, interim
measures were available to suspend or terminate the enforcement during
the substantive assessment. On that basis, the Court concluded that the
legislation in question did not make it excessively difficult for
consumers to rely on protection under the UCTD.

Concerning a transition arrangement for a new right to object to
mortgage enforcement based on the unfairness of contract
terms <a href="#ntr432-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0432"
id="ntc432-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0432">(<span
class="super note-tag">432</span>)</a>, the
Court <a href="#ntr433-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0433"
id="ntc433-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0433">(<span
class="super note-tag">433</span>)</a> held that the four-week period
for forming opposition in relation to pending proceedings was, in
principle, reasonable and
proportionate <a href="#ntr434-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0434"
id="ntc434-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0434">(<span
