class="super note-tag">434</span>)</a>. Nevertheless, the Court
considered that the fact that the affected consumers were informed of
this right only through the official journal of the Member State but not
personally by the relevant
court <a href="#ntr435-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0435"
id="ntc435-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0435">(<span
class="super note-tag">435</span>)</a> created a significant risk that
the time-limit would expire without the consumers' being able to
exercise their rights, which infringed the effectiveness principle and
thereby the UCTD <a href="#ntr436-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0436"
id="ntc436-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0436">(<span
class="super note-tag">436</span>)</a>.

The Court has referred to a two-week time-limit to object to a payment
order based on a promissory note as a ‘brief
period’ <a href="#ntr437-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0437"
id="ntc437-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0437">(<span
class="super note-tag">437</span>)</a>. It considered this period to be
particularly problematic where the defendant has to organise his defence
within these two weeks by submitting all his complaints and adducing
facts and evidence.

The Court <a href="#ntr438-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0438"
id="ntc438-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0438">(<span
class="super note-tag">438</span>)</a> also considered that a period of
15 days combined with the requirement to give reasons for an objection
against a payment order may dissuade a consumer from using this remedy.

<span class="expanded">Service</span>

The fact that the measure or decision that may be challenged is served
on the consumer before the time-limit starts to run provides at least a
minimum guarantee that the consumer is informed about the existence of
the relevant measure of
decision <a href="#ntr439-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0439"
id="ntc439-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0439">(<span
class="super note-tag">439</span>)</a>. The required standard of service
may also be relevant when assessing the risk of consumers' not using
available remedies, along with the information that is provided to
consumers when the document is being served on them.

<span class="expanded">Legal fees and obligation to take a lawyer</span>

Court fees and fees for legal advice and representation can also be, on
their own, a factor deterring consumers from using remedies. Important
is not only the absolute amount, but also, for instance, the ratio with
the value of the claim or their discriminatory character. Lawyers' fees
will have to be taken into account where consumers are formally obliged
to be represented by a lawyer or where there is, at least in practice, a
need to take a lawyer.

Mechanisms aiming to compensate for the consumer's financial
difficulties, such as legal aid, also have to be taken into
account <a href="#ntr440-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0440"
id="ntc440-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0440">(<span
class="super note-tag">440</span>)</a> and may at least reduce the
impact of fees.

The Court <a href="#ntr441-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0441"
id="ntc441-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0441">(<span
class="super note-tag">441</span>)</a> has considered that the
obligation to be represented by a lawyer for cases exceeding a value of
EUR 900 and the associated fees, as a factor which may dissuade
consumers from defending themselves.

The Court <a href="#ntr442-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0442"
id="ntc442-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0442">(<span
class="super note-tag">442</span>)</a> has found that a rule under which
the defendant must pay three quarters of the court fees where he lodges
an objection against an order for payment, to be in itself capable of
deterring a consumer from lodging an objection.

<span class="expanded">Need to justify the use of the remedy</span>

The obligation to make substantial submissions regarding the legal and
factual elements of the case, including evidence, when bringing a
remedy, is liable to dissuade consumers from bringing a remedy,
especially if this is linked to a short
time-limit <a href="#ntr443-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0443"
id="ntc443-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0443">(<span
class="super note-tag">443</span>)</a>. The same applies where consumers
have to indicate reasons for using the remedy for objecting to a payment
order within a period of 15
days <a href="#ntr444-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0444"
id="ntc444-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0444">(<span
class="super note-tag">444</span>)</a>.

Even if there is no formal obligation to take a lawyer, the need to
justify the remedy may create the need to involve a lawyer, which, in
light of the time needed and associated costs, as discussed above, may
be an additional factor dissuading consumers from using the remedy.

<span class="expanded">Availability of interim measures</span>

The Court <a href="#ntr445-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0445"
id="ntc445-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0445">(<span
class="super note-tag">445</span>)</a> has repeatedly stressed the
significance of the availability of interim measures in particular in
order to halt or suspend enforcement against a consumer while the court
is assessing the unfairness of relevant contract terms. Without interim
measures, there is a risk that the protection against unfair contract
terms comes too late and is, therefore, not effective. Interim relief is
particularly important in relation to enforcement concerning the
consumer's home <a href="#ntr446-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0446"
id="ntc446-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0446">(<span
class="super note-tag">446</span>)</a>, involving evictions, but is
relevant also for other enforcement measures. The
Court <a href="#ntr447-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0447"
id="ntc447-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0447">(<span
class="super note-tag">447</span>)</a> has summarised the legal position
as follows:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘44</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>[…]the Court has also held that the
legislation of a Member State does not comply with Directive 93/13
where, while not providing in mortgage enforcement proceedings for
grounds of objection based on the unfairness of a contractual term on
which the right to seek enforcement is based, did not permit the court
before which declaratory proceedings had been brought, which had
jurisdiction to assess the unfairness of such a term, to grant interim
relief, including, in particular, the staying of those enforcement
proceedings […] <a href="#ntr448-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0448"
id="ntc448-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0448">(<span
class="super note-tag">448</span>)</a>.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>45</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Finally, the Court has ruled as being
contrary to Directive 93/13, national legislation which does not allow
the court responsible for the enforcement, in mortgage enforcement
proceedings, either to assess of its own motion or at the consumer's
request, the unfairness of a term contained in the contract which gives
rise to the debt claimed and which constitutes the basis of the right to
enforcement, or to grant interim relief capable of staying or
terminating the mortgage enforcement proceedings, where such relief is
necessary to ensure the full effectiveness of the final decision of the
court hearing the declaratory proceedings before which the consumer
argues that that term is unfair[…] <a
href="#ntr449-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0449"
id="ntc449-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0449">(<span
class="super note-tag">449</span>)</a>.’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Interim measures can be essential not only to suspend enforcement
against consumers, but also in cases where consumers take legal action
to request a declaration of invalidity of certain contract
terms <a href="#ntr450-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0450"
id="ntc450-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0450">(<span
class="super note-tag">450</span>)</a>.

Article 7(1) may also require that national courts must have the
<span class="italic">possibility to grant interim relief of their own
motion</span>, where

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the grant of such relief is necessary in order to ensure the full effectiveness of a later judgment involving unfair contract terms |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | and where there is a significant risk that consumers will not request interim measures <a href="#ntr451-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0451"
id="ntc451-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0451">(<span
class="super note-tag">451</span>)</a>. |

Finally, <span class="italic">not only the complete absence of interim
measures may infringe the effectiveness of remedies, but also the fact
that it is difficult for consumers to obtain interim relief</span> in
light of, for instance, tight time-limits, submissions to be made or
securities or evidence to be provided.

<span class="expanded">Lack of knowledge and information</span>

Consumers will often be unaware of their rights or may not appreciate
the extent of their rights, or may find it difficult to assess the legal
situation because of the limited information provided to them, for
instance, in a payment order which they may
oppose <a href="#ntr452-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0452"
id="ntc452-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0452">(<span
class="super note-tag">452</span>)</a>. Lack of knowledge or limited
information can create the risk that consumers will not use available
remedies <a href="#ntr453-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0453"
id="ntc453-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0453">(<span
class="super note-tag">453</span>)</a>. The Court
confirmed <a href="#ntr454-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0454"
id="ntc454-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0454">(<span
class="super note-tag">454</span>)</a> that the information provided to
consumers in the decision that may be challenged or in connection with
it is vital. This will include information on the fact that the act can
be challenged, as well as on what grounds it can be challenged and in
what form, and on the relevant time-limit. Furthermore, limited
information on the substance of the claim may make it difficult for
consumers to judge the chances of success when challenging certain acts,
such as payment orders. It is not impossible either that, depending on
its content, the information provided to consumers can dissuade
consumers from using the available remedies.

So far, the Court <a href="#ntr455-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0455"
id="ntc455-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0455">(<span
class="super note-tag">455</span>)</a> has given only a few indications
as to how a significant risk that consumers will not bring remedies
based on lack of knowledge or information can be established. In any
event, the examination of this risk will have to take into account the
typical situation of consumers, including vulnerable consumers, in the
type of procedure in question.

<span class="expanded"> <span class="italic">Res judicata</span> and
limitation periods in general</span>

Like time-limits, limitation periods and the rules on the finality of
decisions of courts or other bodies (<span class="italic">res
judicata</span>) are linked to the principle of legal certainty. While
both limitation periods and <span class="italic">res judicata</span> are
legal obstacles for bringing proceedings, <span class="italic">res
judicata</span> may also have the effect that a court is prevented from
(re-)considering certain questions of substantive law, including at the
appeal or enforcement stage, whether on the request of a party or of the
court's own motion.

Despite the fact that <span class="italic">res judicata</span> and
limitation periods may, in some circumstances, be at odds with ‘material
justice’, the Court has recognised the value of legal certainty in the
legal order of the Union and the Member States. On this basis, the
Court <a href="#ntr456-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0456"
id="ntc456-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0456">(<span
class="super note-tag">456</span>)</a> has confirmed that the
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> of consumer law does not, in
principle, require setting aside the domestic rules on
<span class="italic">res judicata</span> and reasonable time-limits,
including limitation periods:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>‘68</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>[…] it is true that the Court has also
recognised that consumer protection is not absolute. In particular, it
has ruled to the effect that EU law does not require a national court to
disapply domestic rules of procedure conferring finality on a decision,
even if to do so would make it possible to remedy an infringement of a
provision, regardless of its nature, contained in Directive 93/13 […] <a
href="#ntr457-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0457"
id="ntc457-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0457">(<span
class="super note-tag">457</span>)</a>.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>69</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Likewise, the Court has previously held that
in the interests of legal certainty it is compatible with EU law to lay
down reasonable time-limits for bringing proceedings […] <a
href="#ntr458-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0458"
id="ntc458-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0458">(<span
class="super note-tag">458</span>)</a>.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>70</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Nevertheless, the application of a
procedural rule, such as a reasonable limitation period, is to be
distinguished from a temporal limitation of the effects of an
interpretation of a rule of EU law. […]’</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

—   <span class="expanded"> <span class="italic">Res judicata</span>
</span>

In light of these findings of the Court, the principle of
<span class="italic">res judicata</span> will generally prevail in cases
that have been completed by a final court decision which can no longer
be challenged. This applies even if that decision infringed the UCTD
and/or where the jurisprudence on the assessment of a specific type of
contract term has changed.

However, it is still necessary to examine whether the specific
<span class="italic">res judicata</span> rule in question
disproportionately or excessively limits remedies or prevents
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> control in relation to the
unfairness of contract terms.

As explained under Section 5.4.1, a national rule on
<span class="italic">res judicata</span> <span class="bold">will not be
compatible with the <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> principle
where it prevents <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control of
contract terms before a claim is enforced</span> against a consumer
while there are no effective remedies or there is a significant risk
that consumers will not use the available
remedies <a href="#ntr459-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0459"
id="ntc459-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0459">(<span
class="super note-tag">459</span>)</a>. Likewise, the
Court <a href="#ntr460-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0460"
id="ntc460-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0460">(<span
class="super note-tag">460</span>)</a> has ruled that, if a court has
examined only some relevant contract terms, <span class="italic">res
judicata</span> cannot prevent the assessment of additional contract
terms at a later stage, whether at the consumer's request or of the
court's own motion:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘Thus, in the case where, in a previous examination of a contract in
dispute which led to the adoption of a decision which has become res
judicata, the national court limited itself to examining of its own
motion, with regard to Directive 93/13, one or certain terms of that
contract, that directive requires a national court, […], before which a
consumer has properly lodged an objection to enforcement proceedings, to
assess, at the request of the parties or of its own motion where it is
in possession of the legal and factual elements necessary for that
purpose, the potential unfairness of other terms of that contract. In
the absence of such a review, consumer protection would be incomplete
and insufficient and would not constitute either an adequate or
effective means of preventing the continued use of that term, contrary
to Article 7(1) of Directive 93/13 […] <a
href="#ntr461-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0461"
id="ntc461-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0461">(<span
class="super note-tag">461</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Furthermore, as discussed in Section 5.3.1, courts may be obliged to
assess the unfairness of contract terms of their own motion under the
<span class="italic">equivalence</span>
principle <a href="#ntr462-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0462"
id="ntc462-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0462">(<span
class="super note-tag">462</span>)</a> where national rules of procedure
empower the courts to examine matters of public policy despite an
otherwise applicable <span class="italic">res judicata</span> rule.

—   <span class="expanded"> <span class="italic">Limitation
periods</span> </span>

As stated above, the Court <a href="#ntr463-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0463"
id="ntc463-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0463">(<span
class="super note-tag">463</span>)</a>, in principle, considers
reasonable limitation periods to be acceptable in the interests of legal
certainty, for instance, in relation to claims for reimbursement of
overpaid amounts based on unfair contract terms. So far, the Court has
not decided what would constitute a reasonable limitation period in that
respect and has not ruled on its starting point. However, the Court has
been asked to provide guidance on the latter
aspect <a href="#ntr464-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0464"
id="ntc464-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0464">(<span
class="super note-tag">464</span>)</a>.

A distinction has to be made between limitation periods laid down in law
and the temporal limitation of the effects of a court's ruling on the
unfairness of a contract term and the associated
consequences <a href="#ntr465-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0465"
id="ntc465-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0465">(<span
class="super note-tag">465</span>)</a>, such as the entitlement of
consumers to reimbursement <a href="#ntr466-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0466"
id="ntc466-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0466">(<span
class="super note-tag">466</span>)</a>, such temporal limitation being
inadmissible <a href="#ntr467-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0467"
id="ntc467-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0467">(<span
class="super note-tag">467</span>)</a>.

In any event, as stated in Section 4.2, the non-binding character of
unfair contract terms cannot in itself be subject to limitation periods.
This implies that consumers can always rely on this protection when
confronted with claims from sellers or suppliers based on unfair
contract terms, either by invoking unfairness themselves or by way of
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> control, without being
time-barred <a href="#ntr468-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0468"
id="ntc468-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0468">(<span
class="super note-tag">468</span>)</a>. The same must apply to requests
to declare contract terms unfair in individual proceedings or
injunctions within the meaning of Article 7(2) UCTD.

5.5.   <span class="bold">What does <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> control imply?</span>

5.5.1.   <span class="italic">Fundamental obligations</span>

<span class="italic">Ex officio</span> control requires a pro-active
intervention by national courts, independently of the parties'
submissions <a href="#ntr469-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0469"
id="ntc469-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0469">(<span
class="super note-tag">469</span>)</a>, both regarding

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the examination as to whether a relevant contract term is unfair and, therefore, non-binding, and |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | the consequences to be drawn from a finding that the term in question is unfair in order to ensure that the consumer is not bound by that term. |

National courts may apply unfair terms only if, exceptionally, a
consumer, who has been informed of his rights, opposes the
non-application of unfair contract
terms <a href="#ntr470-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0470"
id="ntc470-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0470">(<span
class="super note-tag">470</span>)</a>. The Court has stated that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[t]he national court is required to examine, of its own motion, the
unfairness of a contractual term where it has available to it the legal
and factual elements necessary for that task. Where it considers such a
term to be unfair, it must not apply it, except if the consumer opposes
that non-application. […] <a href="#ntr471-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0471"
id="ntc471-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0471">(<span
class="super note-tag">471</span>)</a>.</p>
<p>The full effectiveness of the protection provided for by the
directive requires the national court that has found of its own motion
that a term is unfair to be able to establish all the consequences of
that finding, without expecting the consumer, who has been fully
informed of his rights, to submit a statement requesting that that term
be declared invalid [… <a href="#ntr472-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0472"
id="ntc472-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0472">(<span
class="super note-tag">472</span>)</a>] <a
href="#ntr473-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0473"
id="ntc473-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0473">(<span
class="super note-tag">473</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The obligation of <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control may
also require judges to order interim measures of their own motion where
this is necessary for the effectiveness of the remedy and where there is
a significant risk that consumers may not apply for interim
relief <a href="#ntr474-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0474"
id="ntc474-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0474">(<span
class="super note-tag">474</span>)</a>.

Moreover, judges are obliged to inform the parties on the outcome of the
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> assessment of a contract term and
the conclusions to be drawn so that they can be heard on this
question <a href="#ntr475-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0475"
id="ntc475-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0475">(<span
class="super note-tag">475</span>)</a>.

5.5.2.   <span class="italic">Aspects to be examined</span>

The duty for national courts to assess the unfairness of contract terms
of their own motion requires that they examine all prerequisites for
finding a term unfair <a href="#ntr476-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0476"
id="ntc476-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0476">(<span
class="super note-tag">476</span>)</a>, including, insofar as the
individual steps are necessary under the relevant national
transposition, the questions of

<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>whether the contract terms falls with the scope
of the Directive <a href="#ntr477-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0477"
id="ntc477-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0477">(<span
class="super note-tag">477</span>)</a>, which requires that</p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>there is is a contract between a seller or
supplier and a consumer <a href="#ntr478-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0478"
id="ntc478-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0478">(<span
class="super note-tag">478</span>)</a>,</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>the term in question was not individually
negotiated <a href="#ntr479-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0479"
id="ntc479-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0479">(<span
class="super note-tag">479</span>)</a>,</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 96%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"><p>—</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>the term in question does not reflect mandatory
rules within the meaning of Article 1 (2);</p></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | whether or not the contract term is covered by Article 4(2) and if that is the case, whether it fulfils the transparency requirements; |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | whether the contract term is unfair, i.e. whether, contrary to the requirement of good faith, it creates a significant imbalance in the rights and obligations of the parties to the detriment of the consumer, including a possible lack of transparency of the relevant terms, or, where appropriate, whether it corresponds to one of the terms contained in a black or grey list. |

5.5.3.   <span class="italic">Availability of the necessary legal and
factual elements</span>

The main element for the assessment of the unfairness of contract terms
is the contract with all its terms. However, as explained above, before
the unfairness of a term is to be assessed other elements have to be
considered, e.g. whether one party is a consumer, whether a term was
negotiated individually, or whether a seller or supplier provided any
necessary information to the consumer before the conclusion of the
contract.

One problem may be that, in a given case, the national court may not
have all necessary factual and legal elements in order to rule on the
unfairness of a contract term. The Court acknowledges this when
employing in many rulings formulations such as ‘where \[the national
court\] has available to it the necessary legal and factual
elements’ <a href="#ntr480-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0480"
id="ntc480-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0480">(<span
class="super note-tag">480</span>)</a>.

At the same time, the Court has made it clear that
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> control implies a pro-active
approach to obtain access to the elements necessary for the assessment
of contract terms <a href="#ntr481-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0481"
id="ntc481-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0481">(<span
class="super note-tag">481</span>)</a>, for instance when it used the
term ‘investigate’ in relation to examining the prerequisites of Article
3(1) UCTD <a href="#ntr482-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0482"
id="ntc482-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0482">(<span
class="super note-tag">482</span>)</a>:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…], the Court has held that that national court <span
class="bold">must investigate of its own motion whether a term in a
contract concluded between a seller or supplier and a consumer falls
within the scope of the Directive and</span>, if it does, assess of its
own motion whether such a term is unfair […] <a
href="#ntr483-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0483"
id="ntc483-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0483">(<span
class="super note-tag">483</span>)</a>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The Court <a href="#ntr484-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0484"
id="ntc484-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0484">(<span
class="super note-tag">484</span>)</a> has also emphasised that national
courts have to obtain the necessary clarifications for the question of
whether one party is a consumer if there is at least some indication
that this may be the case:

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…], the principle of effectiveness requires a national court before
which a dispute relating to a contract which may be covered by that
directive has been brought to determine whether the purchaser may be
classified as a consumer, even if the purchaser has not expressly
claimed to have that status, as soon as that court has at its disposal
the matters of law and of fact that are necessary for that purpose <span
class="bold">or may have them at its disposal simply by making a request
for clarification</span>.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Therefore, if there are indications that the relevant contract may be a
consumer contract, a national court is under the obligation to
investigate this issue even if the parties have not raised it. Such
pro-active approach would indeed appear to be required by the mandatory
character of Article 6(1).

Likewise, if there are indications that a claim may be based on contract
terms which were not negotiated individually, but not all elements are
immediately available to complete this examination, national courts will
have to raise this question with the parties in order to obtain the
necessary clarifications and
evidence <a href="#ntr485-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0485"
id="ntc485-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0485">(<span
class="super note-tag">485</span>)</a>. Where sellers or suppliers were
obliged to provide specific information to consumers, courts have to
check whether consumers received the required
information <a href="#ntr486-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0486"
id="ntc486-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0486">(<span
class="super note-tag">486</span>)</a>.

Insofar as particular rules of procedure, e.g. in payment order or
enforcement proceedings, do not allow the courts to make a substantive
assessment despite the availability of those
elements <a href="#ntr487-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0487"
id="ntc487-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0487">(<span
class="super note-tag">487</span>)</a> or do not give them access to
those elements <a href="#ntr488-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0488"
id="ntc488-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0488">(<span
class="super note-tag">488</span>)</a>, including the contract on which
the claim is based, such procedural restrictions cannot remove the
obligation to ensure <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control.

This interpretation is supported by the following considerations:

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | Already the formulation used by the Court and the context of the different rulings suggest that the Court acknowledges the <span class="italic">fact</span> that, <span class="italic">in practice</span>, it will not be possible for a national court to carry out the necessary assessment without access to those elements <a href="#ntr489-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0489"
id="ntc489-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0489">(<span
class="super note-tag">489</span>)</a>. |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | In the majority of cases the Court took into account that the referring court did have access to the necessary elements. Furthermore, in several of those rulings, the Court used the formulation <span class="italic">‘… <span class="bold">even when/even though</span> </span> <a href="#ntr490-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0490"
id="ntc490-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0490">(<span
class="super note-tag">490</span>)</a> <span class="italic">it has available to it the matters of law and fact necessary to that end …’</span>, indicating an <span class="italic">a fortiori</span> reasoning as opposed to a legal condition. |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | If national rules of procedure could prevent <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control simply by denying courts access to the necessary elements, this would undermine the right to an effective remedy. |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| — | Where <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control is required because of the <span class="italic">equivalence</span> principle, such control could be prevented in practice where national rules of procedure deny the courts access to the necessary elements. |

The Court <a href="#ntr491-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0491"
id="ntc491-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0491">(<span
class="super note-tag">491</span>)</a> confirmed this interpretation,
when, after establishing the existence of a significant risk that
consumers will not lodge an objection to a payment
order <a href="#ntr492-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0492"
id="ntc492-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0492">(<span
class="super note-tag">492</span>)</a>, it considered that the issuing
of a payment order without any prior <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> examination of the unfairness of contract terms was
incompatible with Article 7(1) UCTD. The Court came to this conclusion
although it was aware that, under the relevant rules of procedure,
national courts did normally not have access to the legal and factual
elements for this
examination <a href="#ntr493-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0493"
id="ntc493-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0493">(<span
class="super note-tag">493</span>)</a> and without mentioning access to
such elements as a condition for its
finding <a href="#ntr494-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0494"
id="ntc494-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0494">(<span
class="super note-tag">494</span>)</a>, when it stated that

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><div>
<p>‘[…] Article 7(1) of Directive 93/13 must be interpreted as
precluding a procedure which authorises the issue of an order for
payment where the court dealing with an application for an order for
payment does not have the power to examine whether the terms of that
agreement are unfair, if the detailed rules for exercising the right to
lodge an objection against such an order do not enable observance of the
rights which the consumer derives from that directive to be
ensured.’</p>
</div></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Therefore, in cases where the UCTD requires <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> control, national courts must be obliged to obtain the
necessary elements for the <span class="italic">ex officio</span>
assessment by interpreting national rules in conformity of EU law or, if
that is not possible, by, setting aside the conflicting national rules.

5.5.4.   <span class="italic">Conclusions to be drawn from the
unfairness assessment</span>

At the end of the assessment, national courts have to draw the
consequences stemming from the unfairness of relevant contract terms and
their non-binding character, in compliance with the principles set out
in Section 4. Depending on the claim, the type of procedure and the
nature of the contract term, this could lead, for instance, to the
rejection or limitation of a claim against a consumer that is fully or
partially based on unfair contract terms or the termination or
limitation of the enforcement, or a declaration of invalidity.

As stated above, before a national court decides to disapply a contract
term which it has assessed of its own motion and which it considers to
be unfair, it has to hear both parties on this
question <a href="#ntr495-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0495"
id="ntc495-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0495">(<span
class="super note-tag">495</span>)</a>.

Furthermore, consumers may decide not to rely on this protection in
court proceedings after having been informed of the unfair nature and
the non-binding character of the contract terms in question, in which
the competent will have to apply the unfair contract
term <a href="#ntr496-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0496"
id="ntc496-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0496">(<span
class="super note-tag">496</span>)</a>. In light of the mandatory
character of Article 6(1) UCTD, such declaration should be valid only
where the judge is satisfied that the consumer has fully understood the
legal situation and that their declaration is not based on
misconceptions or pressure by other parties.

5.6.   <span class="bold">Implications of <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> control, <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> and
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> for the national rules of
procedure</span>

Where EU law requires <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control of
the unfairness of contract terms, national courts have to ensure such
control by interpreting and applying national law as much as possible in
conformity with EU law <a href="#ntr497-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0497"
id="ntc497-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0497">(<span
class="super note-tag">497</span>)</a>. Where this is impossible and
national rules of procedure do not comply with the principle of
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> and/or do not guarantee an
effective remedy, national courts have to set such national rules aside
in order to carry out the <span class="italic">ex officio</span> checks
required by EU law <a href="#ntr498-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0498"
id="ntc498-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0498">(<span
class="super note-tag">498</span>)</a>.

Moreover, the principles of <span class="italic">ex officio</span>
control and <span class="italic">effectiveness</span> may require the
Member States to make certain adaptations or corrections in their
legislation insofar as national rules of procedure and substance are in
conflict with these principles as described in the sub-sections above.
The Member States are, therefore, invited to examine all national
provisions that may be in conflict with the guarantees required by the
UCTD as interpreted by the Court.

Where <span class="italic">ex officio</span> control is required by the
<span class="italic">equivalence</span>
principle <a href="#ntr499-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0499"
id="ntc499-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0499">(<span
class="super note-tag">499</span>)</a>, national courts are obliged to
apply the relevant national provisions <span class="italic">mutatis
mutandis</span> in order to assess the unfairness of contract terms of
their own motion. However, if those provisions do not explicitly cover
actions based on EU law, there is a risk that national courts may fail
to carry out this control based solely on those national provisions.
Therefore, compliance with the <span class="italic">equivalence</span>
principle may require legislative adaptations as well.

Finally, the Court <a href="#ntr500-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0500"
id="ntc500-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0500">(<span
class="super note-tag">500</span>)</a> has clarified that a decision by
a national court adjudicating at last instance which does not comply
with its obligation to assess the unfairness of contract terms of its
own motion may constitute a sufficiently serious infringement of EU law
that could trigger the liability of the Member State for damages caused
to consumers.

5.7.   <span class="bold"> <span class="italic">Ex officio</span>
control of unfair contract terms and out-of-court proceedings</span>

The case law of the Court on procedural guarantees stemming from the
UCTD is addressed exclusively to ‘courts and tribunals’ within the
meaning of Article 267 TFEU. The Court has held that arbitral tribunals
cannot make preliminary
references <a href="#ntr501-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0501"
id="ntc501-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0501">(<span
class="super note-tag">501</span>)</a>.

With regard to the UCTD, the
Court <a href="#ntr502-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0502"
id="ntc502-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0502">(<span
class="super note-tag">502</span>)</a> ruled that its case law on the
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> duty for national courts does not
apply to notaries when affixing the enforcement clause to an authentic
document. However, assessment of the procedure in its entirety may take
into account the role of notaries, under the relevant national law, when
drawing up such documents <a href="#ntr503-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0503"
id="ntc503-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0503">(<span
class="super note-tag">503</span>)</a>. At the same time, guarantees in
the pre-judicial phase cannot replace access to a full judicial
assessment by a judge <a href="#ntr504-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0504"
id="ntc504-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0504">(<span
class="super note-tag">504</span>)</a>.

However, the Court has clarified that, in connection with arbitration
proceedings initiated by traders against consumers, insofar as they are
admissible under the applicable national law, there must be effective
judicial control of arbitration awards in appeal and enforcement
proceedings <a href="#ntr505-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0505"
id="ntc505-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0505">(<span
class="super note-tag">505</span>)</a>. Based on the
<span class="italic">equivalence</span> and
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> <a href="#ntr506-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0506"
id="ntc506-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0506">(<span
class="super note-tag">506</span>)</a> principles, this may imply an
obligation for the courts to assess of their own motion the unfairness
of relevant contract terms, including of contract terms allowing the
seller or supplier to resort to arbitration, if necessary at the
enforcement stage. National rules on such proceedings that jeopardise
the application of the principle of effective judicial protection
against unfair contract terms must be considered as contrary to the
UCTD <a href="#ntr507-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0507"
id="ntc507-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0507">(<span
class="super note-tag">507</span>)</a>. Contract terms under which
traders may impose arbitration proceedings on consumers are likely to be
unfair if they exclude or hinder the consumer's right to take legal
action or exercise a legal
remedy <a href="#ntr508-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0508"
id="ntc508-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0508">(<span
class="super note-tag">508</span>)</a>, including where they prevent
effective judicial control of unfair contract terms.

Regarding dispute settlement procedures initiated by consumers,
Directive 2013/11/EU on alternative dispute resolution (ADR) for
consumer disputes <a href="#ntr509-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0509"
id="ntc509-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0509">(<span
class="super note-tag">509</span>)</a> contains important guarantees
<span class="italic">inter alia</span> for agreements between a consumer
and a trader to submit complaints to an ADR entity, as well as for the
fairness and legality of proceedings before recognised ADR-entities.
Within the scope of Directive 2013/11/EU, an agreement between a
consumer and a trader to submit complaints to an ADR entity is not
binding on the consumer prior to a dispute if it has the effect of
depriving the consumer of his right to bring an action before the courts
for the settlement of the dispute. This applies <span class="italic">a
fortiori</span> where such agreement is contained in a not-individually
negotiated contract term.

6.   <span class="bold">INJUNCTIONS IN THE COLLECTIVE INTEREST OF
CONSUMERS (ARTICLE 7(2) AND (3) UCTD)</span>

<table class="borderOj" width="100%" data-border="1"
data-cellspacing="0" data-cellpadding="10">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><p><span class="italic">Article 7</span></p>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>1.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>Member States shall ensure that, in the
interests of consumers and of competitors, adequate and effective means
exist to prevent the continued use of unfair terms in contracts
concluded with consumers by sellers or suppliers.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>2.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>The means referred to in paragraph 1 shall
include provisions whereby persons or organizations, having a legitimate
interest under national law in protecting consumers, may take action
according to the national law concerned before the courts or before
competent administrative bodies for a decision as to whether contractual
terms drawn up for general use are unfair, so that they can apply
appropriate and effective means to prevent the continued use of such
terms.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<table width="100%" data-border="0" data-cellspacing="0"
data-cellpadding="0">
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 4%" />
<col style="width: 92%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td data-valign="top"></td>
<td data-valign="top"><p>3.</p></td>
<td data-valign="top"><span>With due regard for national laws, the legal
remedies referred to in paragraph 2 may be directed separately or
jointly against a number of sellers or suppliers from the same economic
sector or their associations which use or recommend the use of the same
general contractual terms or similar terms.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

Article 7(2) and (3) UCTD complement Directive 2009/22/EC on injunctions
for the protection of consumers'
interests <a href="#ntr510-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0510"
id="ntc510-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0510">(<span
class="super note-tag">510</span>)</a> with particular regard to
injunctions in the collective interest of consumers in order to prevent
the continued use of unfair contract terms by individual sellers or
suppliers or groups of them. Pursuant to Article 7(1), also injunction
proceedings have to be adequate and
effective <a href="#ntr511-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0511"
id="ntc511-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0511">(<span
class="super note-tag">511</span>)</a>. In light of the deterrent and
dissuasive purpose of such actions, as well as their independence from
any particular dispute, authorised persons or organisations, such as
consumer associations, may bring injunction proceedings even though the
relevant terms have not yet been used in specific
contracts <a href="#ntr512-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0512"
id="ntc512-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0512">(<span
class="super note-tag">512</span>)</a>. Conversely, the Court held that
Article 7(1) and (2) and Article 47 of the Charter do not oblige Member
States to allow a consumer organisation to intervene in support of
individual consumers in proceedings concerning the enforcement of
potentially unfair contract
terms <a href="#ntr513-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0513"
id="ntc513-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0513">(<span
class="super note-tag">513</span>)</a>, unless this is required by the
<span class="italic">equivalence</span>
principle <a href="#ntr514-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0514"
id="ntc514-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0514">(<span
class="super note-tag">514</span>)</a>.

The principles of <span class="italic">equivalence</span> and
<span class="italic">effectiveness</span> and <span class="italic">ex
officio</span> control, as well as Article 47 of the Charter, apply
equally to injunctions in the collective interests of consumers, while
their particular nature has to be taken into account.

In particular, Article 6(1), read in conjunction with Article 7(1) and
(2), requires that contract <span class="bold">terms which are declared
unfair in an action for an injunction are neither binding on the
consumers who are parties to the action nor on those who have concluded
with the same seller or supplier a contract to which the same terms
apply</span> <a href="#ntr515-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0515"
id="ntc515-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0515">(<span
class="super note-tag">515</span>)</a>. A term deemed to be unfair in
such a procedure is considered to be unfair also in all future contracts
between that trader and
consumers <a href="#ntr516-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0516"
id="ntc516-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0516">(<span
class="super note-tag">516</span>)</a>. <span class="bold">National
courts</span> adjudicating individual cases are obliged to take this
effect of injunctions into account as part of their
<span class="italic">ex officio</span> duties and <span class="bold">may
not consider the relevant term to be fair and valid.</span>

The Court has also recognised, in principle, the possibility to increase
protection against unfair contract terms under Article 8 by creating a
national register of contract terms found to be unfair in final court
rulings based on which an enforcement authority may fine also
<span class="italic">other sellers or suppliers</span> using equivalent
terms. However, in light of Article 47 of the Charter, such sellers or
suppliers have to have an effective judicial remedy against the decision
declaring the terms to be equivalent and against the decision fixing the
amount of the fine <a href="#ntr517-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0517"
id="ntc517-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0517">(<span
class="super note-tag">517</span>)</a>.

Despite the clear benefits of collective actions under Article 7(2),
such actions must not undermine the right of consumers who bring
parallel individual actions seeking a declaration of the unfairness of a
contract term to dissociate themselves from the collective action
regarding similar terms used in contracts of the same type. As explained
by the Court <a href="#ntr518-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0518"
id="ntc518-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0518">(<span
class="super note-tag">518</span>)</a>, individual and collective
actions under the UCTD are complementary and have different purposes and
legal effects. A collective action for an injunction aims at the
abstract, general assessment of whether a contract term is unfair,
whereas an individual action entails a specific examination of the
contract term in the light of the particular circumstances of the
case <a href="#ntr519-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0519"
id="ntc519-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0519">(<span
class="super note-tag">519</span>)</a>. Consequently, collective actions
can have only a limited procedural impact on individual actions,
justified in particular by the sound administration of justice and the
need to avoid incompatible judicial decisions. Thus, Article 7 precludes
a national rule requiring a court automatically to suspend an individual
action brought before it by a consumer until a final judgment in a
parallel collective action brought by an association is handed
down <a href="#ntr520-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0520"
id="ntc520-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0520">(<span
class="super note-tag">520</span>)</a>.

In this context, interim measures should be available within the
individual action, both at the consumer's request and of the court's own
motion, for as long as appropriate, pending a final judgment in an
ongoing collective action <a href="#ntr521-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0521"
id="ntc521-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0521">(<span
class="super note-tag">521</span>)</a>. This is relevant especially when
interim relief is necessary to ensure the full effectiveness of the
judgment in the individual action.

Regarding jurisdiction rules, the Court has accepted that a national
rule under which actions for an injunction brought by consumer
protection associations must be brought before the courts where the
defendant, i.e. the seller or supplier, is established or has its
address do not infringe the effectiveness
principle <a href="#ntr522-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0522"
id="ntc522-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0522">(<span
class="super note-tag">522</span>)</a>. The Court considered that
consumer associations are not in the same weak position as individual
consumers when requesting injunctions against sellers or suppliers, and
referred to Article 4(1) of Directive
2009/22/EC <a href="#ntr523-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0523"
id="ntc523-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0523">(<span
class="super note-tag">523</span>)</a>.

The Court has further held that the Union rules on jurisdiction mean
that an action for an injunction brought by a consumer protection
association for the purpose of preventing a trader from using terms
considered to be unfair in contracts with private individuals is a
matter relating to tort, delict or quasi-delict within the meaning of
the Brussels Convention <a href="#ntr524-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0524"
id="ntc524-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0524">(<span
class="super note-tag">524</span>)</a>. That interpretation is valid
also for the Brussels I
Regulation <a href="#ntr525-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0525"
id="ntc525-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0525">(<span
class="super note-tag">525</span>)</a>. This implies that the
jurisdiction can be attributed to a court where the harmful event took
place, which is to be understood broadly with regard to consumer
protection, covering not only situations where an individual has
personally sustained damage but also, in particular, the undermining of
legal stability by the use of unfair
terms <a href="#ntr526-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0526"
id="ntc526-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0526">(<span
class="super note-tag">526</span>)</a>. The law applicable to such an
action must be determined in accordance with Article 6(1) of the Rome II
Regulation <a href="#ntr527-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0527"
id="ntc527-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0527">(<span
class="super note-tag">527</span>)</a>, whereas the law applicable to
the assessment of a particular contractual term must always be
determined pursuant to the Rome I
Regulation <a href="#ntr528-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0528"
id="ntc528-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0528">(<span
class="super note-tag">528</span>)</a>, whether that assessment is made
in an individual action or in a collective
action <a href="#ntr529-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0529"
id="ntc529-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0529">(<span
class="super note-tag">529</span>)</a>.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

class="super">49</span>)</a>  The Court may provide further
clarifications on this category of contracts in Case C-272/18
<span class="italic">Verein für Konsumenteninformation v TVP Treuhand-
und Verwaltungsgesellschaft für Publikumsfonds mbH & Co KG</span>
(pending on 31 May 2019) concerning fiduciary agreements concluded
between a managing partner and other limited partners in a limited
partnership under German law.

<a href="#ntc50-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0050"
id="ntr50-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0050">(<span
class="super">50</span>)</a>  As expressed by the Advocate General in
paragraph 56 of Case C-590/17 <span class="italic">Pouvin Dijoux</span>,
Recital 10 ‘provides illustrative examples of types of legal
transactions that will fall short of Article 1(1) read in conjunction
with Article 2(b) and (c) of the directive’.

<a href="#ntc51-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0051"
id="ntr51-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0051">(<span
class="super">51</span>)</a>  See, with regard to employment contracts,
Case C-590/17 <span class="italic">Pouvin Dijoux</span>, paragraph 32.

<a href="#ntc52-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0052"
id="ntr52-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0052">(<span
class="super">52</span>)</a>  Cases C-74/15 <span class="italic">Dumitru
Tarcău</span> and C-534/15 <span class="italic">Dumitraş.</span>

<a href="#ntc53-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0053"
id="ntr53-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0053">(<span
class="super">53</span>)</a>  This is confirmed by the common position
of national authorities within the network of enforcement authorities
created under Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 of the European Parliament
and of the Council of 27 October 2004 on cooperation between national
authorities responsible for the enforcement of consumer protection laws
(the Regulation on consumer protection cooperation) ([OJ L 364,
9.12.2004, p.
1](./../../../../legal-content/EN/AUTO/?uri=OJ:L:2004:364:TOC))
concerning the protection of consumers on social networks available at
http://europa.eu/rapid/press-release_IP-17-631_en.htm (November 2016).
See also the concept of remuneration according to Recital 16 of
Directive (EU) 2018/1972 of the European Parliament and of the Council
of 11 December 2018 establishing the European Electronic Communications
Code ([OJ L 321, 17.12.2018, p.
36](./../../../../legal-content/EN/AUTO/?uri=OJ:L:2018:321:TOC)).

<a href="#ntc54-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0054"
id="ntr54-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0054">(<span
class="super">54</span>)</a>  Cases C-74/15 <span class="italic">Dumitru
Tarcău</span>, paragraph 26 and C-534/15
<span class="italic">Dumitraş</span>, paragraph 31.

<a href="#ntc55-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0055"
id="ntr55-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0055">(<span

<a href="#ntc57-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0057"
id="ntr57-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0057">(<span
class="super">57</span>)</a>  Case C-110/14
<span class="italic">Costea.</span>

<a href="#ntc58-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0058"
id="ntr58-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0058">(<span
class="super">58</span>)</a>  Case C-147/16 <span class="italic">Karel
de Grote.</span>

<a href="#ntc59-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0059"
id="ntr59-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0059">(<span
class="super">59</span>)</a>  Cases C-74/15 <span class="italic">Dumitru
Tarcău</span> and C-534/15 <span class="italic">Dumitraş.</span>

<a href="#ntc60-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0060"
id="ntr60-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0060">(<span
class="super">60</span>)</a>  Case C-590/17 <span class="italic">Pouvin
Dijoux</span>. The Court held that the notion of ‘consumer’ under
Article 2(b) of the Directive covers the employee of an undertaking and
his spouse, who conclude a loan contract with that undertaking,
reserved, principally, to members of staff of that undertaking, with a
view to financing the purchase of real estate for private purposes. The
notion of ‘seller or supplier’ under Article 2(c) of the Directive
covers such an undertaking, where it concludes such a loan contract in
the context of its professional activity, even if granting loans does
not constitute its main activity.

<a href="#ntc61-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0061"
id="ntr61-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0061">(<span
class="super">61</span>)</a>  In some Member States (see Annex II), also
contract terms that have been negotiated individually are subject to the
rules on unfair contract terms.

<span class="italic">Kušionová</span>, paragraph 77.

Med</span>, paragraph 33, C-446/17 <span class="italic">Woonhaven
Antwerpen</span>, paragraph 25, C-186/16
<span class="italic">Andriciuc</span>, paragraph 29; C-280/13
<span class="italic">Barclays Bank</span>, paragraphs 31 and 42, C-34/13
<span class="italic">Kušionová</span>, paragraph 77, C-92/11
<span class="italic">RWE Vertrieb</span>, paragraph 26.

<a href="#ntc71-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0071"
id="ntr71-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0071">(<span
class="super">71</span>)</a>  Case C-51/17 <span class="italic">OTP Bank
v Ilyés and Kiss</span>, paragraph 53, Case C-92/11
<span class="italic">RWE Vertrieb</span>, paragraph 28. See also Recital
13 of the UCTD.

<a href="#ntc72-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0072"
id="ntr72-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0072">(<span
class="super">72</span>)</a>  Case C-51/17 <span class="italic">OTP Bank
v Ilyés and Kiss</span>, paragraphs 62-64. However, such provision may
not deprive consumers of the rights they may derive from invalidity of
the contract because of the unfair nature of a contract term. See
Section 4.3.2.1 and Case C-118/17 <span class="italic">Dunai</span>,
paragraphs 51-55.

<a href="#ntc73-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0073"
id="ntr73-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0073">(<span
class="super">73</span>)</a>  In Case C-51/17 <span class="italic">OTP
Bank v Ilyés and Kiss,</span> the Court found that a standard contract
term relating to the foreign exchange <span class="italic">risk</span>
in a foreign-currency denominated mortgage loan agreement is not
excluded from the scope of the UCTD, even if national law contains
mandatory provisions on the currency conversion mechanism.

<a href="#ntc74-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0074"
id="ntr74-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0074">(<span
class="super">74</span>)</a>  Case C-92/11 <span class="italic">RWE
Vertrieb</span>, point 1 of the operative part: ‘Article 1(2) of Council
Directive 93/13/EEC of 5 April 1993 on unfair terms in consumer
contracts must be interpreted as meaning that that directive applies to
provisions in general terms and conditions, incorporated into contracts
concluded between a supplier and a consumer, which reproduce a rule of
national law applicable to another category of contracts and are not
subject to the national legislation concerned.’.

exclude the application of the UCTD in relation to contract terms
concerning pricing.

<a href="#ntc108-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0108"
id="ntr108-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0108">(<span
class="super">108</span>)</a>  See Common position of national
authorities within the CPC network concerning the protection of
consumers on social networks (November 2016),
http://europa.eu/rapid/press-release_IP-17-631_en.htm.

<a href="#ntc109-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0109"
id="ntr109-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0109">(<span
class="super">109</span>)</a>  Article 8a was added through Article 32
of Directive 2011/83/EU on consumer rights ([OJ L 304, 22.11.2011, p.
64](./../../../../legal-content/EN/AUTO/?uri=OJ:L:2011:304:TOC)).

<a href="#ntc110-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0110"
id="ntr110-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0110">(<span
class="super">110</span>)</a>  This is confirmed, for instance, in
paragraph 55 of Case C-191/15 <span class="italic">Verein für
Konsumenteninformation v Amazon</span>: ‘\[…\] It must be observed in
this respect that the level of protection of consumers still varies from
one Member State to another, in accordance with Article 8 of Directive
93/13, so that the assessment of a term may vary, other things being
equal, according to the applicable law.’ The Court also confirmed this
in Case C-453/10 <span class="italic">Pereničová and Perenič</span>.

<a href="#ntc111-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0111"
id="ntr111-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0111">(<span
class="super">111</span>)</a>  Inserted through Article 32 of Directive
2011/83/EU ([OJ L 304, 22.11.2011, p.
64](./../../../../legal-content/EN/AUTO/?uri=OJ:L:2011:304:TOC)).

<a href="#ntc112-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0112"
id="ntr112-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0112">(<span
class="super">112</span>)</a>  The notified national rules can be found
in Annex II and at:

https://ec.europa.eu/info/notifications-under-article-8a-directive-93-13-eec_en

<a href="#ntc113-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0113"
id="ntr113-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0113">(<span
class="super">113</span>)</a>  Mentioned explicitly in Article 8a(1)
first indent.

<a href="#ntc114-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0114"
id="ntr114-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0114">(<span
class="super">114</span>)</a>  The latter two instances are not
mentioned explicitly in Article 8a.

<a href="#ntc115-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0115"
id="ntr115-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0115">(<span
class="super">115</span>)</a>  In paragraph 61 of Case C-143/13
<span class="italic">Matei and Matei</span>, the Court confirmed that a
‘black list’ of terms to be regarded as unfair is one of the more
stringent measures that Member States may adopt or retain in the area
covered by the UCTD to ensure a maximum degree of protection for the
consumer which is compatible with EU law.

<a href="#ntc116-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0116"
id="ntr116-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0116">(<span
class="super">116</span>)</a>  See also Section 4, where the
relationship between transparency and unfairness is described.

<a href="#ntc117-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0117"
id="ntr117-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0117">(<span
class="super">117</span>)</a>  Case C-484/08 <span class="italic">Caja
de Ahorros y Monte de Piedad de Madrid</span>, paragraphs 41–44.

<a href="#ntc118-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0118"
id="ntr118-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0118">(<span
class="super">118</span>)</a>  The function of Article 4(2) is further
explained in Section 3.1.

<a href="#ntc119-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0119"
id="ntr119-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0119">(<span
class="super">119</span>)</a>  Joined Cases C-96/16 and C-94/17
<span class="italic">Banco Santander Escobedo Cortés</span>, paragraph
69.

<a href="#ntc120-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0120"
id="ntr120-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0120">(<span
class="super">120</span>)</a>  Joined Cases C-96/16 and C-94/17
<span class="italic">Banco Santander Escobedo Cortés</span>, paragraphs
62-71; Case C-118/17 <span class="italic">Dunai</span>, paragraphs
60-64.

<a href="#ntc121-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0121"
id="ntr121-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0121">(<span
class="super">121</span>)</a>  [OJ L 149, 11.6.2005, p.
22](./../../../../legal-content/EN/AUTO/?uri=OJ:L:2005:149:TOC).

<a href="#ntc122-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0122"
id="ntr122-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0122">(<span
class="super">122</span>)</a>  Article 6(1) is discussed in detail in
Section 4 and, as regards its procedural implications, in Section 5.

<a href="#ntc123-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0123"
id="ntr123-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0123">(<span
class="super">123</span>)</a>  E.g. in Case C-453/10
<span class="italic">Pereničová and Perenič.</span>

<a href="#ntc124-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0124"
id="ntr124-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0124">(<span
class="super">124</span>)</a>  See Case C-453/10
<span class="italic">Pereničová and Perenič</span>, paragraphs 46 and 47
in fine:

|  |  |
|----|----|
| ‘46 | Consequently, a finding that a commercial practice is unfair has no direct effect on whether the contract is valid from the point of view of Article 6(1) of Directive 93/13. |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| 47 | \[…\] A finding that such a commercial practice is unfair is one element among others on which the competent court may, pursuant to Article 4(1) of Directive 93/13, base its assessment of the unfairness of the contractual terms relating to the cost of the loan granted to the consumer. Such a finding, however, has no direct effect on the assessment, from the point of view of Article 6(1) of Directive 93/13, of the validity of the credit agreement concluded.’ |

<a href="#ntc125-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0125"
id="ntr125-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0125">(<span
class="super">125</span>)</a>  Case C-453/10
<span class="italic">Pereničová and Perenič</span>, paragraph 35:
‘Directive 93/13 does not therefore preclude a Member State from laying
down, in compliance with European Union law, national legislation under
which a contract concluded between a trader and a consumer which
contains one or more unfair terms may be declared void as a whole where
that will ensure better protection of the consumer.’

<a href="#ntc126-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0126"
id="ntr126-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0126">(<span
class="super">126</span>)</a>  E.g. Joined Cases C-482/13, C-484/13,
C-485/13 and C-487/13 <span class="italic">Unicaja Banco</span>,
paragraph 38.

<a href="#ntc127-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0127"
id="ntr127-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0127">(<span
class="super">127</span>)</a>  Reference to Case C-26/13
<span class="italic">Kásler and Káslerné Rábai</span>, paragraph 64.

<a href="#ntc128-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0128"
id="ntr128-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0128">(<span
class="super">128</span>)</a>  Case 106/77
<span class="italic">Simmenthal</span>, paragraphs 21-26 The principles
established in <span class="italic">Simmenthal</span> have been
confirmed, for instance, in Case C-689/13
<span class="italic">PFE</span>, paragraphs 40 and 41:

|  |  |
|----|----|
| ‘40 | A national court which is called upon, within the exercise of its jurisdiction, to apply provisions of EU law is under a duty to give full effect to those provisions, if necessary refusing of its own motion to apply any conflicting provision of national legislation, even if adopted subsequently, and it is not necessary for the court to request or await the prior setting aside of such provision by legislative or other constitutional means \[…\]’ (reference to Case 106/77 <span class="italic">Simmenthal,</span> paragraphs 21 and 24, and, Case C-112/13 <span class="italic">A</span>, paragraph 36). |

|  |  |
|----|----|
| ‘41 | Any provision of a national legal system and any legislative, administrative or judicial practice that might impair the effectiveness of EU law by withholding from the national court with jurisdiction to apply such law the power to do everything necessary at the moment of its application to set aside national legislative provisions that might prevent EU rules from having full force and effect are incompatible with those requirements, which are the very essence of EU law \[…\]’ (reference to Case 106/77 <span class="italic">Simmenthal,</span> paragraph 22, and Case C-112/13 <span class="italic">A</span>, paragraph 37). |

<a href="#ntc129-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0129"
id="ntr129-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0129">(<span
class="super">129</span>)</a>  Case C-118/17
<span class="italic">Dunai</span>, paragraph 61.

<a href="#ntc130-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0130"
id="ntr130-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0130">(<span
class="super">130</span>)</a>  This is implied by Case C-168/15
<span class="italic">Milena Tomášová</span>, where the Court ruled that,
under certain conditions, the Member States are liable to compensate
consumers for damage caused by the fact that a court adjudicating at
last instance did not assess relevant contract terms of its own motion
although it was required to do so under the UCTD, even if there was no
explicit rule in that respect in national law. Cases C-618/10
<span class="italic">Banco Español de Crédito,</span> C-49/14
<span class="italic">Finanmadrid</span>, C-176/17
<span class="italic">Profi Credit Polska</span> and C-632/17
<span class="italic">PKO</span> are examples where the Court found that
national courts were required to assess the unfairness of contract terms
of their own motion even though national law did not provide for such
assessment. The question of <span class="italic">ex officio</span>
control of the unfairness of contract terms is discussed in detail in
Section 5.

<a href="#ntc131-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0131"
id="ntr131-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0131">(<span
class="super">131</span>)</a>  The relationship between the UCTD and
national rules of procedure is discussed specifically in Section 5
below.

<a href="#ntc132-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0132"
id="ntr132-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0132">(<span
class="super">132</span>)</a>  In Case C-144/99
<span class="italic">Commission v Netherlands</span>, paragraph 21, the
Court stressed the requirement of legal certainty in connection with the
transposition of the UCTD.

<a href="#ntc133-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0133"
id="ntr133-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0133">(<span
class="super">133</span>)</a>  In Case C-143/13
<span class="italic">Matei and Matei</span>, paragraph 60, the Court
refers to the Annex as a ‘grey list’. However, there may be certain
variations in the understanding of the term ‘grey list’ in the
transposition of the Member States, which may include a merely
indicative list as in the Annex to the UCTD, but also a legal
presumption that the listed terms are unfair.

<a href="#ntc134-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0134"
id="ntr134-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0134">(<span
class="super">134</span>)</a>  The third sentence of Article 5, however,
deviates from this principle in relation to collective procedures aiming
to prevent the continuous use of a contract term (see also Case C-70/03
<span class="italic">Commission v Spain</span>, paragraph 16).

<a href="#ntc135-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0135"
id="ntr135-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0135">(<span
class="super">135</span>)</a>  However, where Member States have opted
not to transpose this requirement, the national authorities may assess
the possible unfairness of the main-subject-matter or of the price or
remuneration even if the relevant contract terms are presented in a
clear and intelligible manner. See Case C-484/08
<span class="italic">Caja de Ahorros Monte de Piedad de Madrid</span>,
paragraphs 40-44.

<a href="#ntc136-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0136"
id="ntr136-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0136">(<span
class="super">136</span>)</a>  Case C-472/10
<span class="italic">Invitel</span>, point 1 of the operative part and
paragraphs 30 and 31; Case C-226/12 <span class="italic">Constructora
Principado</span>, paragraph 27.

<a href="#ntc137-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0137"
id="ntr137-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0137">(<span
class="super">137</span>)</a>  Case C-191/15 <span class="italic">Verein
für Konsumenteninformation v Amazon</span>, point 2 of the operative
part and paragraphs 65-71.

<a href="#ntc138-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0138"
id="ntr138-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0138">(<span
class="super">138</span>)</a>  Since Case C-237/02
<span class="italic">Freiburger Kommunalbauten.</span>

<a href="#ntc139-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0139"
id="ntr139-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0139">(<span
class="super">139</span>)</a>  The citation is from Case C-243/08
<span class="italic">Pannon GSM,</span> paragraphs 42 and 43. Similar
language can be found, for instance in Case C-421/14
<span class="italic">Banco Primus</span>, paragraph 57; Case C-415/11
<span class="italic">Aziz</span>, paragraph 66 and the case law cited
there; Case C-226/12 <span class="italic">Constructora
Principado</span>, paragraph 20 Case C-472/10
<span class="italic">Invitel</span>, paragraph 22 and Case C-237/02

and the operative part.

<a href="#ntc140-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0140"

on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).

<a href="#ntc141-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0141"

<span class="italic">Caja de Ahorros y Monte de Piedad de Madrid</span>,

<a href="#ntc147-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0147"
id="ntr147-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0147">(<span
class="super">147</span>)</a>  Where Member States have not transposed
this limitation contained in Article 4(2) UCTD into their national law
(See Annex II to this Notice), the unfairness of such terms, including
the adequacy of the price, can be assessed regardless of any lack of
transparency. In Case C-484/08 <span class="italic">Caja de Ahorros
Monte de Piedad</span> the Court confirmed that such national
transposition is covered by Article 8. In point 1 of the operative part
the Court stated: ‘Articles 4(2) and 8 of Council Directive 93/13/EEC
\[…\] must be interpreted as <span class="italic">
<span class="bold">not</span> </span> precluding national legislation,
\[…\], which authorises a judicial review as to the unfairness of
contractual terms which relate to the definition of the main
subject-matter of the contract or to the adequacy of the price and
remuneration, on the one hand, as against the services or goods to be
supplied in exchange, on the other hand, even in the case where those
terms are drafted in plain, intelligible language.’

<a href="#ntc148-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0148"
id="ntr148-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0148">(<span
class="super">148</span>)</a>  Regarding the main-subject-matter of the
contract.

<a href="#ntc149-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0149"
id="ntr149-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0149">(<span
class="super">149</span>)</a>  Excluding an assessment of the adequacy
of the price or remuneration.

<a href="#ntc150-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0150"
id="ntr150-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0150">(<span
class="super">150</span>)</a>  Case C-186/16
<span class="italic">Andriciuc</span>, paragraph 34; Case C-26/13
<span class="italic">Kásler and Káslerné Rábai</span>, paragraph 42, and
Case C-96/14 <span class="italic">Van Hove</span>, paragraph 31. The
Court has been asked to provide further interpretation on this matter in
Case C-84/19 <span class="italic">Credit Profi Polska</span> (pending on
31 May 2019).

<span class="italic">Lupean,</span> paragraph 17.

<a href="#ntc161-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0161"

class="super">161</span>)</a>  Case C-119/17,
<span class="italic">Lupean,</span> paragraphs 18-21.

<a href="#ntc162-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0162"
id="ntr162-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0162">(<span
class="super">162</span>)</a>  Case C-26/13 <span class="italic">Kásler
and Káslerné Rábai.</span>

<a href="#ntc163-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0163"
id="ntr163-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0163">(<span
class="super">163</span>)</a>  National law may give courts the
possibility to assess the adequacy of the price even where such terms
are clear and intelligible (See Annex II to this Notice).

<a href="#ntc164-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0164"
id="ntr164-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0164">(<span
class="super">164</span>)</a>  E.g. Case C-143/13
<span class="italic">Matei and Matei</span>, paragraph 56.

<a href="#ntc165-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0165"
id="ntr165-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0165">(<span
class="super">165</span>)</a>  Case C-472/10
<span class="italic">Invitel</span>, paragraph 23.

<a href="#ntc166-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0166"
id="ntr166-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0166">(<span
class="super">166</span>)</a>  In Case C-472/10
<span class="italic">Invitel</span>, paragraph 24, the Court went on to
say: ‘With regard to a contract term providing for an amendment of the
total price of the service provided to the consumer, it should be
pointed out that, in light of points 1(j) and (l) and 2(b) and (d) of
the annex to the Directive, the reason for and the method of the
variation of the aforementioned price must, in particular, be set out,
the consumer having the right to terminate the contract.’

<a href="#ntc167-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0167"
id="ntr167-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0167">(<span
class="super">167</span>)</a>  Case C-143/13 <span class="italic">Matei
and Matei</span>, in particular paragraph 47. Furthermore, the fact that
a fee does not correspond to an actual service means that its assessment
would not concern the adequacy of that fee, paragraph 70.

<a href="#ntc168-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0168"
id="ntr168-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0168">(<span
class="super">168</span>)</a>  Case C-26/13 <span class="italic">Kásler
and Káslerné Rábai</span>, paragraphs 57 and 58.

<a href="#ntc169-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0169"
id="ntr169-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0169">(<span
class="super">169</span>)</a>  Case C-26/13 <span class="italic">Kásler
and Káslerné Rábai</span>, paragraph 58, confirmed, for instance, in
Case C-143/13 <span class="italic">Matei and Matei</span>, paragraph 70.

<a href="#ntc170-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0170"
id="ntr170-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0170">(<span
class="super">170</span>)</a>  Unless the national transposition applies
also to contract terms that have been negotiated individually (See Annex
II to this Notice).

<a href="#ntc171-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0171"
id="ntr171-C_2019323EN.01000401-E0171">(<span

<span class="italic">Andriciuc</span>, paragraph 46; Case C-143/13
<span class="italic">Matei and Matei</span>, paragraph 75; Case C-26/13
